These techniques take practice, but they can be mastered by anyone. Next, we’ll go further. I’ll show you how to become a complete, undisputed expert in the mind of any listener in about ninety seconds.

那天我处境极其不利，但我还是成功吸引了最后一位投资人的注意力，因为我努力传递了一个令人信服的地位信息，并达成了地位共识。这些技巧需要练习，但任何人都能掌握。接下来，我们将更进一步。我将向你展示如何在短短九十秒内，让任何听众都对你刮目相看，成为一位无可争议的专家。

#↩

CHAPTER 3

第三章

Creating Certainty

创造确定性

I always knew Project Windbreak was going to be a tough deal because The Swiss had a reputation for being tough dealmakers, and yet we flew straight to Switzerland and booked ourselves a meeting with a group of the most tightly wound, risk-averse investment bankers in Geneva in hopes of convincing them to invest in my client, a six-foot-four-inch, four-hundred-pound Steelers fan from Columbus, Ohio, named Billy whose only knowledge of local Swiss culture came from drinking Swiss Miss hot chocolate, which was the sister brand of his other favorite food, Slim Jim (both produced by the Conagra company of Chicago). So no, he did not “fit in” here.

我一直都知道“防风林项目”会是一笔棘手的交易，因为瑞士人以精明强干著称。然而，我们还是直接飞到瑞士，在日内瓦约见了一群最谨慎、最保守的投资银行家，希望能说服他们投资我的客户——一个身高六英尺四英寸、体重四百磅的俄亥俄州哥伦布市的匹兹堡钢人队球迷，名叫比利。他对瑞士当地文化的了解仅限于喝瑞士小姐热巧克力，而瑞士小姐热巧克力和他最爱的另一种食品——瘦肉棒（Slim Jim）——是姐妹品牌（两者都由芝加哥的康尼格拉公司生产）。所以，他在这里确实“格格不入”。

Yet here we were, Geneva, Switzerland, about as far from Columbus as you can get, ready to sell Billy’s cutting-edge cybersecurity technology, codename Project Windbreak.

然而，我们却身处瑞士日内瓦，离哥伦布市非常遥远，准备出售比利的尖端网络安全技术，代号“防风工程”。

We had come to Geneva at my suggestion for one reason only: a full one-third of the world’s private money is stockpiled here in 350 separate “family offices,” as they are called.

在我的建议下，我们来到日内瓦只有一个原因：世界上三分之一的私人资金都储存在这里的 350 个独立的“家族办公室”中。

Our plan was simple (and probably naive). We would meet with a venerated boutique Swiss bank on their own turf and convince them to invest in our company, take a place on the board of directors, and then use their prestigious brand name and reputation to reassure other, larger European institutions that it was safe to roll the dice on us and Windbreak.

我们的计划很简单（或许也很天真）。我们会去一家备受尊敬的瑞士精品银行，在他们的地盘上与他们会面，说服他们投资我们的公司，让他们进入董事会，然后利用他们享有的盛誉和声誉，让其他规模更大的欧洲机构相信，投资我们和Windbreak是安全的。

I was staying at the Four Seasons des Bergues, situated on the banks of the Rhône River, in the heart of the city, with a view of the perpetually snowcapped French Alps. It was the only place in town where I wouldn’t starve to death—I need more than just the Swiss staple foods of bread, cheese, and alcohol to survive when I’m on the road.

我住在贝尔格四季酒店，它坐落在罗纳河畔，位于市中心，可以远眺终年积雪的法国阿尔卑斯山。这是城里唯一一个我不会饿死的地方——在路上，光靠瑞士人常吃的面包、奶酪和酒是活下去的。

Billy, however, is a lot less picky. His favorite meal, he cheerfully explained to me on the flight over, consists of pepperoni bread, a deep-fried corned beef hash and sauerkraut sandwich, and something he called the Buckeye dog, the details of which I begged him not to share with me. Suffice it to say, Billy would give five stars to just about anything on a plate as long as there was a lot of it—which there never was in Geneva unless you bought three of the same thing. And three of anything in Geneva will cost you a lot more than you want to pay, no matter what it is.

然而，比利就没那么挑剔了。在飞往日内瓦的航班上，他兴高采烈地告诉我，他最爱的食物包括意大利辣香肠面包、炸咸牛肉杂烩酸菜三明治，以及一种他称之为“七叶树热狗”的东西——我恳求他不要告诉我这东西的具体做法。总之，只要分量足，比利几乎什么都给五星好评——但在日内瓦，除非你买三份一样的，否则根本吃不饱。而且，在日内瓦，不管是什么，三份的价格都会远远超出你的预期。

As a result, Billy was staying down the road at the Hotel President Wilson, which served the largest all-you-can-eat breakfast in town. But that wasn’t the only reason Billy picked the Wilson. It’s well known in cybersecurity circles that back in 2016 during a summit at the UN, the Hotel President Wilson’s computer servers were infected by a spy virus and wound up recording some highly sensitive conversations between world leaders. After this incident, the place became legendary among cybersecurity geeks—and Billy was definitely one of those. Billy had been working at a top-ranked university to develop the next generation of voice ID systems for financial institutions and other top-secret entities such as the FBI, DOD, and NSA. Billy’s latest offering was designed specifically for banking centers, where hundreds of millions of dollars were moved with a single phone call. He had created a way to not only ensure the caller was who they said they were, but to measure the stress level in the voice to determine the emotional circumstances surrounding the transaction and guarantee it was being made voluntarily. That may not matter when you’re transferring a hundred dollars to your kid’s college debit card, but when $1 million is leaving your account headed directly to some random company in the Cayman Islands, verification is something you care about—a lot.

因此，比利下榻在附近的威尔逊总统酒店，这家酒店提供全城最丰盛的自助早餐。但这并非比利选择威尔逊酒店的唯一原因。网络安全圈内人尽皆知，2016年联合国峰会期间，威尔逊总统酒店的服务器曾感染间谍病毒，导致一些世界领导人之间的高度敏感对话被录下。事件发生后，这家酒店在网络安全极客圈内声名鹊起——而比利无疑是其中一员。比利一直在顶尖大学工作，致力于为金融机构和其他高度机密机构（例如联邦调查局、国防部和国家安全局）开发下一代语音识别系统。比利最新的产品是专门为银行中心设计的，在这些中心，数亿美元的资金只需一个电话就能转移。他开发了一种方法，不仅可以确保来电者的身份属实，还可以测量语音中的压力水平，从而判断交易时的情绪状态，并确保交易是自愿进行的。如果你只是往孩子的大学借记卡里转一百美元，那可能无关紧要；但是，如果一百万美元要从你的账户直接汇往开曼群岛的某个不知名公司，那么验证就成了你非常关心的事情。

Our first meeting was with one of the oldest, most prestigious banks in Geneva, that had practically invented the concept of Swiss banking and the reputation for Swiss certainty. They were exactly who we needed as partners and investors.

我们第一次会面的对象是日内瓦一家历史悠久、声名卓著的银行，这家银行几乎可以说是瑞士银行业的奠基人，也是瑞士信誉卓著的缔造者。他们正是我们需要的合作伙伴和投资者。

I had pulled up to the President Wilson at precisely seven-thirty, which left us thirty minutes to get to our meeting at the bank headquarters on the Boulevard Helvétique, check in, and be announced by eight a.m. on the dot, a full hour before I usually wake up.

我七点半准时到达威尔逊总统酒店，这样我们就有三十分钟的时间赶到位于赫尔维蒂克大道上的银行总部开会，办理登记手续，并在早上八点准时被宣布到场，这比我通常起床的时间早了整整一个小时。

It was now 7:41 and still no sign of Billy. I was getting ready to go inside and pull him away from the breakfast table when he came rushing out the front doors balancing three croissants in one hand and two different-sized mugs of hot chocolate in the other. He climbed in the car and handed me one of the mugs.

当时是7点41分，比利还没出现。我正准备进屋把他从餐桌旁拉开，这时他突然从前门冲了出来，一只手拿着三个羊角面包，另一只手端着两杯大小不同的热巧克力。他上了车，递给我一杯热巧克力。

“It’s Nestlé,” he said, as if that was something rare and exotic, then added with glee, “It’s complimentary!”

“是雀巢的，”他说，仿佛那是什么稀有而奇特的东西，然后高兴地补充道，“是免费的！”

“You think you have enough food rations to survive the fifteen-minute ride?” I asked Billy, who stopped for a moment and looked like he wasn’t sure he did. Before he could rush back to the buffet for another few thousand calories, I told the driver to get on the gas—we had some time to make up.

“你觉得你带的食物够撑过这十五分钟的车程吗？”我问比利，他停顿了一下，看起来不太确定。还没等他冲回自助餐台再补充几千卡路里，我就让司机加速——我们得抓紧时间赶路。

Fortunately, the United Nations wasn’t in session, so traffic was light. In less than fifteen minutes we pulled onto a circular gravel driveway and into a parking lot where rows of luxury sedans and a few Ferraris waited patiently for their owners to emerge. A valet opened Billy’s door and he climbed out with his hot chocolate (the croissants were long gone).

幸好联合国休会，所以路上车不多。不到十五分钟，我们就驶入一条环形碎石车道，进入一个停车场。停车场里停着一排排豪华轿车和几辆法拉利，静静地等待着车主出现。一位代客泊车员打开了比利的车门，他拿着热巧克力下了车（羊角面包早就被吃光了）。

“You cannot take that in,” I said firmly. The valet nodded in agreement. “I’ll take that for you, sir,” he said in an impeccable English accent.

“你不能带进去，”我斩钉截铁地说。侍者点头表示同意。“先生，我来帮您拿吧，”他用一口流利的英国腔说道。

We entered the lobby, which echoed our footsteps and turned our conversation into whispers the way cathedrals do. It was massive, with flagstone and steel and a wall of glass through which I could see acres of bucolic Swiss countryside with the Alps looming in the distance, just beyond the morning fog that rose daily off Lake Geneva and melted away like pixie dust by noon.

我们走进大厅，脚步声在回荡，如同置身教堂一般，我们的谈话也变成了低语。大厅气势恢宏，以石板和钢铁为主要建材，一面玻璃墙外，我可以看到绵延数英亩的瑞士田园风光，远处阿尔卑斯山脉巍峨耸立，晨雾每日从日内瓦湖升起，到中午便如仙尘般消散。

“Can I get you a cappuccino?” the receptionist asked us. Billy, of course, nodded. I was just glad he didn’t ask for four of them—and a doughnut.

“请问您要一杯卡布奇诺吗？”前台接待员问我们。比利当然点了点头。我庆幸他没要四杯——外加一个甜甜圈。

A man I presumed to be our host, Monsieur Lustenberger, materialized in the lobby, made his way over to us, and, nodding crisply, offered his hand. “Monsieur Klaff, Monsieur Campbell, thank you so much for coming. Right this way, please.”

我猜想是我们的主人卢斯滕贝格先生，他出现在大厅里，朝我们走过来，干脆地点了点头，伸出手。“克拉夫先生，坎贝尔先生，非常感谢你们的到来。这边请。”

We followed as Lustenberger led us to an exquisite executive conference room where three men who looked just like him stood up at attention as we approached the table. They were all thin and tall and taciturn, in suits so flawless and skillfully tailored you couldn’t even imagine where you would find such clothing—and even if you could, it still would never look right on you. You had to be a Swiss banker to do a suit like that justice.

我们跟随卢斯滕贝格尔来到一间精致的高级会议室。我们走近桌边时，三个和他长得一模一样的男人笔直地站了起来。他们都身材修长，沉默寡言，穿着剪裁完美无瑕的西装，你根本无法想象这种衣服从何而来——即便你能找到，穿在自己身上也未必合适。只有瑞士银行家才能驾驭这样的西装。

“You have come a very long way to visit us,” said Monsieur Philippe, as we were introduced. “How can we be of service?”

“你们远道而来拜访我们，真是辛苦了，”菲利普先生在介绍我们时说道，“我们能为您做些什么呢？”

Of course, by offering to “be of service,” these men were really asking us in the characteristically Swiss way: Why are you and Professor Pepperoni Pie over here shopping your investment opportunity in Geneva when you should be shopping closer to home, like Palo Alto or San Jose? Did you strike out there and come to us thinking we wouldn’t know about it because we’re nestled in the Alps?

当然，这些人说着“乐意效劳”，其实是在用典型的瑞士式方式质问我们：你和“意大利辣香肠派教授”为什么要在日内瓦推销你们的投资机会，而不是在离家更近的地方，比如帕洛阿尔托或圣何塞？你们是不是在那儿碰壁了，以为我们身处阿尔卑斯山脚下，肯定不知道你们的投资机会？

Just as I had suspected, this was going to require a bit of script flipping or this meeting would quickly be over. What little these men knew about our company was enough to pique their interest and schedule this meeting—barely—but they were clearly far from sold. It was our move, and we had to make it count. When you’re in a situation like this, being met right off the bat with skepticism—albeit very polite Swiss skepticism—you have to play your cards exactly right.

正如我所料，这次会议需要我们稍微改变一下策略，否则很快就会结束。这些人对我们公司了解甚少，但足以引起他们的兴趣并安排这次会议——虽然勉强——他们显然远未真正信服。现在轮到我们行动了，我们必须把握住机会。在这种情况下，一开始就遭遇怀疑——尽管是彬彬有礼的瑞士式怀疑——你必须谨慎行事，步步为营。

Billy was about to speak; the next words out of his mouth would either make us a $10 million deal or get us run out of there. Luckily, we’d been rehearsing this moment for weeks, and he knew exactly what to say. It was time for a Status Tip-Off. But he’d need more than just Status Alignment to instill certainty in these bankers. Billy needed something else. He was going to have to say something that would cement his position as an absolute authority in his field (cybersecurity) in the mind of every person in the room.

比利正要开口；他接下来说的话要么会让我们签下1000万美元的合同，要么会让我们被赶出去。幸运的是，我们为此演练了好几个星期，他知道该说什么。是时候展现他的地位了。但仅仅依靠地位上的一致性还不足以让这些银行家们信服。比利还需要别的。他必须说些话，才能在在场的每个人心中巩固他在网络安全领域绝对权威的地位。

FILLING THE CERTAINTY GAP

填补确定性差距

Building a dream home is something many people look forward to for years. But the moment you’re ready to make it happen, the first thing you’ll realize—before you can get anyone to do a lick of work—is the number of people who start asking you to write checks. Very large checks.

建造梦想家园是许多人多年来的夙愿。但当你真正准备动手时，首先你会意识到——在你还没来得及让任何人开始动工之前——就会有很多人开始要求你开支票。而且是数额巨大的支票。

To start with, you’re going to need an architect, and it’s going to take a few bucks up front to get a good one. The architect wants you to pay immediately, and sends you an invoice for something like fifty thousand dollars, due upon receipt. In other words, show me the money.

首先，你需要一位建筑师，而要找到一位优秀的建筑师，前期需要支付一笔不小的费用。建筑师会要求你立即付款，并给你寄来一张大约五万美元的账单，要求你收到后立即支付。换句话说，就是“先把钱给我”。

Pay the whole thing up front? Are you crazy? I could get ripped off!

一次性付清？你疯了吗？我怕被骗！

This is the classic transactional puzzle: In a given deal, how much financial risk should you accept? How much risk should you expect the other party to accept?

这是一个经典的交易难题：在特定的交易中，你应该承担多大的财务风险？你期望对方承担多大的风险？

To sort this out, you talk to the architect about payment terms and come to the following arrangement: You’ll pay 50 percent of the total in advance and 50 percent when the plans are delivered. Yes, he can harm you by taking your advance payment and not completing the work as agreed. But you can also harm him by not paying the final bill. So “half now, half later” is a very useful form of a “mutually assured destruction.”

为了解决这个问题，你和建筑师商量付款方式，达成如下协议：你预付总额的50%，剩余50%在图纸交付后支付。没错，他可以收了预付款却不按约定完成工作，从而损害你的利益。但你也可以通过拒付尾款来损害他的利益。所以，“先付一半，后付一半”是一种非常有效的“相互确保毁灭”的策略。

Does this work? Absolutely.

这方法有效吗？绝对有效。

In the United States, this simple formula is responsible for at least $1 trillion of deals each year: 50 percent in advance and 50 percent upon receipt.

在美国，这种简单的公式每年至少促成 1 万亿美元的交易：50% 预付，50% 货到付款。

What’s happening below the surface is that you and the architect are both seeking enough certainty to go ahead with the deal. You each want the sense of control—that you’re not at the mercy of the other party.

表面之下，你和建筑师都在寻求足够的确定性来推进这笔交易。你们都想要掌控感——不想受制于对方。

Our ancient ancestors didn’t have to deal with this problem because before the invention of money, they relied on a system of equal barter. An ancient man from Mesopotamia might have traded a hammer tool to another man from Babylon for a stone pot. These men would not have trusted each other—but it didn’t matter. There was always complete certainty in bartering situations because each party immediately got the thing he wanted. In direct barter, you’re never left waiting and hoping the other party will follow through on their end of the deal.

我们的远古祖先不必面对这个问题，因为在货币发明之前，他们依靠的是等价物交换。一位来自美索不达米亚的古人可能会用一把锤子从巴比伦换取一个石罐。这些人彼此并不信任——但这无关紧要。物物交换总是充满确定性，因为每一方都能立即得到自己想要的东西。在直接物物交换中，你永远不会苦苦等待，祈祷对方履行承诺。

But in today’s world, when you’re selling an idea, a company, or a service to be performed in the future, the buyer can no longer hold it in their hand and have it immediately when the deal is done. Today, almost every transaction has inherent uncertainty, which makes us uncomfortable because our brains are wired to expect the type of total certainty our species experienced for a million years in equal barter.

但在当今世界，当你出售的是一种理念、一家公司或一项未来将要提供的服务时，买家再也无法在交易完成后立即拥有它。如今，几乎每一笔交易都存在固有的不确定性，这让我们感到不安，因为我们的大脑天生就期待着那种我们人类在百万年的等价物交换中体验过的完全确定性。

Whenever your mind desires 100 percent certainty about a deal but your gut says it’s got a 50 percent chance to work out, you’re mentally experiencing a Certainty Gap. The larger this gap gets, the more you will start to feel psychological anxiety and even physical distress—causing you to back away from the deal.

当你的大脑渴望对一笔交易百分之百确定，而你的直觉却告诉你它只有50%的成功几率时，你就正经历着心理上的“确定性差距”。这个差距越大，你就会开始感到越焦虑，甚至出现生理上的不适——最终导致你放弃这笔交易。

Every decision we face, every purchase we make, and every agreement we enter is a function of great uncertainty.

我们面临的每一个决定、我们进行的每一次购买、我们达成的每一项协议都充满了巨大的不确定性。

When a buyer says, “I need to think about it,” at the end of a presentation (a common response), it means they don’t have enough certainty and confidence to move forward. Sure, what you’ve promised sounds good, but what if it doesn’t work out as you say? How can the buyer be sure?

当买家在演示结束时说“我需要考虑一下”（这是一种常见的反应），这意味着他们没有足够的确定性和信心来推进下一步。当然，你承诺的内容听起来不错，但如果实际情况与你所说的不符呢？买家如何才能确信呢？

The goal of every sales presentation is to reduce the Certainty Gap in the buyer’s mind—improving the chance of getting to yes.

每一次销售演示的目标都是缩小买家心中的确定性差距，从而提高成交的几率。

THE LINE BETWEEN TOO LITTLE AND TOO MUCH INFORMATION

信息过少与信息过多之间的界限

The standard method for closing the Certainty Gap is to provide the buyer with more information. For example, we could inform him about some of our recent accomplishments, list twenty other firms we’ve worked with, talk at length about our features, things like that.

弥合确定性差距的标准方法是向买家提供更多信息。例如，我们可以向他介绍我们最近的一些成就，列出我们合作过的二十家其他公司，详细介绍我们的产品功能等等。

This is the classic sales script: Give us the money and we will deliver as promised . Look at all these customer logos—and here’s a testimonial, two awards, and a thousand “likes” on social media.

这是经典的销售话术：给我们钱，我们保证按承诺交付。看看这些客户标志——这里还有客户评价、两项奖项，以及社交媒体上的上千个“赞”。

Instilling certainty is different from creating Status Alignment. With Alignment, your goal is to show the buyer you’re similar to them and make them instantly feel like you “get” them—whether you’re talking to a coal miner or a Fortune 500 CEO. To instill certainty, on the other hand, you need to prove that you are a complete, absolute, undisputed authority in your field. So, in Billy’s case, his Status Tip-Off would have to prove to the room full of Swiss bankers that he’s one of them—an experienced and well-connected banking professional. Then, he would need to prove his bona fides as a cybersecurity expert . . . all within two minutes.

建立信任感与建立地位认同截然不同。地位认同的目标是让买家觉得你和他们相似，让他们立刻感受到你的理解——无论你是在和煤矿工人交谈，还是和财富500强企业的CEO对话。而要建立信任感，你需要证明自己是所在领域绝对权威，无可争议。所以，以比利为例，他需要向满屋子的瑞士银行家证明，他也是他们中的一员——一位经验丰富、人脉广泛的银行专业人士。然后，他还需要证明自己是网络安全专家……所有这些都必须在两分钟内完成。

GETTING TO STATUS ALIGNMENT WITH SWISS SKEPTICS

与瑞士怀疑论者达成地位一致

I had spent weeks preparing Billy for this precise moment. What he said in the next couple of minutes would make or break all our efforts. He needed to prove to these men that even though he might look different, eat different foods, and dress differently (Go Buckeyes!), he still belonged in this room; he was a critical part of the banking industry. He needed to create Status Alignment. To start, it was time for a Status Tip-Off.

我花了几个星期的时间，为比利准备这一刻。接下来几分钟他说的话，将决定我们所有努力的成败。他需要向这些人证明，即便他的外表、饮食和穿着都与他们不同（加油，俄亥俄州立大学！），他仍然属于这个房间；他是银行业不可或缺的一部分。他需要建立地位认同。首先，是时候给他一个地位提示了。

He adjusted in his chair and said, “Excuse me.” All eyes turned to Billy. “I like these offices you have here,” he said with a smile. “Beyond impressive. An almost perfect Victorian restoration.”

他调整了一下坐姿，说道：“打扰一下。”所有人的目光都转向了比利。“我喜欢你们这里的办公室，”他笑着说，“真是令人印象深刻。维多利亚时代的修复几乎完美。”

The Swiss nodded appreciatively at the compliment.

瑞士人赞赏地点头表示赞同。

Billy continued, “Then again, when I take a closer look”—his eyes squinted slowly—“it’s not really Victorian, is it? Take this room, for instance, overlooking the gardens. The design of the room is all about proportion and balance, with sash windows, stucco cornices, the six-paneled door. This is older than Victorian, right? It’s way older. There’s a kind of symmetry and grandness . . . hmmm—oh, of course, now I have it. These construction elements were really part of the Georgian era, where wealth and influence were on more conspicuous display.”

比利继续说道：“不过，当我仔细观察时”——他缓缓眯起眼睛——“它其实并不像维多利亚时代的建筑，对吧？就拿这间可以俯瞰花园的房间来说吧。房间的设计注重比例和平衡，有上下推拉窗、灰泥檐口和六扇板门。这比维多利亚时代更古老，对吧？远比维多利亚时代古老得多。它有一种对称和宏伟的感觉……嗯——哦，原来如此，我明白了。这些建筑元素实际上是乔治时代的风格，在那个时代，财富和影响力更加显眼地展现出来。”

He crossed the room as he spoke and gazed out the large windows along the southern wall of the room, overlooking miles of lush countryside, dramatically pausing for ten seconds.

他一边说话一边穿过房间，凝视着房间南墙上的大窗户，俯瞰着绵延数英里的葱郁乡村，戏剧性地停顿了十秒钟。

“I especially admire these windows. With eight large panes there’s more glass in here than modern building codes allow, and I’m sure you know, in the Georgian period, the number of windows in a house denoted how wealthy a family was, because there was a heavy window tax at the time to help fund the war effort.

“我特别喜欢这些窗户。这里有八块大玻璃，比现代建筑规范允许的玻璃量还要多。我相信您也知道，在乔治时代，房子里的窗户数量代表着一个家庭的富裕程度，因为当时要征收重税来资助战争。”

“These windows . . . they’re a signature for you, right? High ceilings, grand views, they add to the feeling of being among the ‘financial elite’ when you’re in this room. It makes me feel like I’m in an iconic Swiss bank—don’t take this the wrong way either; it’s just terrific, I love it!

“这些窗户……它们是您的标志性设计，对吧？高高的天花板，开阔的视野，置身其中，更让人感觉自己身处‘金融精英’之中。这让我感觉自己仿佛置身于一家标志性的瑞士银行——别误会我的意思；这里真的太棒了，我非常喜欢！”

“But the windows, they’re a problem too, aren’t they? Physical security here is obviously impossible . . . anyone with a two-dollar crowbar can waltz right in.”

“但是窗户也是个问题，不是吗？这里的实体安全显然是不可能的……任何人只要拿着两美元的撬棍就能大摇大摆地进来。”

Then Billy struck a dead serious tone, and stared directly at Philippe as he continued. “Guys, there’s no way in hell you’re ISO 2700 cyber compliant under G7 banking regulations, so you can’t trade directly with any financial exchanges, right? NASDAQ, Börse, hell, even SIX Swiss probably won’t take any of your buy-sell activity.”

然后比利语气变得异常严肃，直视着菲利普继续说道：“伙计们，你们根本不可能符合七国集团银行监管规定的ISO 2700网络安全标准，所以你们不能直接与任何金融交易所进行交易，对吧？纳斯达克、慕尼黑证券交易所，甚至瑞士证券交易所可能都不会接受你们的任何买卖交易。”

I had no idea where this was all headed, but I was loving it. By starting with the architecture and history, he was showing he understood these men culturally. Now, with a shift into a discussion of banking transactions, he was aligning his status on a technical and business level.

我完全不知道这一切会如何发展，但我很喜欢。他先从建筑和历史谈起，展现出他对这些人的文化理解。现在，话题转向银行交易，他又在技术和商业层面上确立了自己的地位。

“You have no real ability to secure this—your main office —so you can’t have any high-speed servers on-site, right? They’d be totally exposed!” Billy was not really asking—he knew. “So no servers here. . . . Aha, you’re renting co-location racks at remote sites? That’s a real pain—I estimate your trade times are, what, a hundred milliseconds per cycle?” He chuckled. “A hundred milliseconds is a joke. In Akron, we’re making trades in less than two milliseconds.

“你们根本没办法保护你们总部的安全，所以不可能在本地放高速服务器，对吧？那样的话它们就完全暴露在外了！” 比利其实不是在问——他心里清楚。“所以这里没有服务器……啊哈，你们是在远程站点租用托管机架？那可真是麻烦——我估计你们的交易时间，大概是每周期一百毫秒吧？” 他轻笑了一声。“一百毫秒简直是笑话。在阿克伦，我们交易不到两毫秒就能完成。”

“Look, don’t take this the wrong way. You guys are terrific; I love this grand architecture, the gravel driveway stacked with Ferraris, and the European traditions—and this cappuccino right here is one of the best ever—but let’s get real with each other. When someone in this office puts up an order to buy or sell stock, you have to send it to a remote trading desk two hundred miles from here to process at a millisecond cycle time. You guys must be getting killed with that lag time. I mean, you might as well be placing trade orders from the North Pole.

“听着，别误会我的意思。你们都很棒；我喜欢这里宏伟的建筑，停满法拉利的砾石车道，还有欧洲的传统——而且这杯卡布奇诺也是我喝过最好的之一——但我们得实话实说。当办公室里有人下单买卖股票时，必须把订单发送到两百英里外的远程交易台，以毫秒级的周期进行处理。你们肯定被这延迟折磨得够呛。我的意思是，你们还不如从北极下单呢。”

“Listen, if you’re interested, I could take a look at your setup and recommend a security work-around. You could definitely get your trade times down to inside of ten milliseconds without much work. I bet we can get you into the three-to-five-millisecond range in about a month. But anyway, just a few thoughts; I know we’re here today to talk about my company and our software . . .”

“听着，如果你有兴趣，我可以看看你的系统配置，并推荐一个安全方面的解决方案。你肯定能轻松地把交易时间缩短到十毫秒以内。我估计一个月左右就能达到三到五毫秒。不过，我只是想说几句；我知道我们今天来这里是为了谈谈我的公司和我们的软件……”

Holy crap, that was unbelievable. Billy had just laid down a flawless Status Tip-Off. He had nailed a major pain point for these guys: They were two hundred miles from the nearest banking exchange and were operating without a banking compliance rating—that’s like trying to get into an exclusive New York City nightclub with a note from your mom. They were surely the slowest trader in the region, and in banking, slow means last in line. Not a good way to run a bank.

我的天哪，简直难以置信。比利刚刚完美地揭露了他们的困境。他精准地指出了这些人的一个痛点：他们距离最近的银行交易所足足有两百英里，而且没有银行合规评级——这就像拿着你妈写的便条想进纽约的高级夜总会一样。他们肯定是该地区最慢的交易员，而在银行业，慢就意味着排在最后。这可不是经营银行的好方法。

Philippe stroked his chin thoughtfully. “Yes, this is true,” he admitted. “The trading lag costs us millions every year but we cannot bear to move to another location. It is a tradition that we bank here, in Geneva. This building is a part of our legacy.”

菲利普若有所思地摸了摸下巴。“是的，的确如此，”他承认道。“交易滞后每年给我们造成数百万的损失，但我们无法承受搬迁到其他地方。在日内瓦开设银行是我们的一项传统。这座大楼是我们传承的一部分。”

“I can see why,” I said, jumping into the conversation. “It’s beautiful. Trust me—if anyone can help, it’s Billy. But let’s shift gears for a minute. Let me tell you about the latest technology this guy has been developing.”

“我明白了，”我插话道，“这东西真漂亮。相信我，如果有人能帮上忙，那一定是比利。不过，咱们先换个话题。让我来给你讲讲这家伙最近研发的新技术。”

The four men all nodded, leaning eagerly toward us. I could immediately tell from their body language that Billy had achieved Status Alignment. They were interested and viewed him as a peer. We’d cleared the first hurdle.

四个男人都点了点头，热情地朝我们这边倾身。从他们的肢体语言中，我立刻就能看出比利已经达到了身份认同的标准。他们对比利很感兴趣，把他当成同辈人。我们跨过了第一道坎。

But now things were going to get more difficult. It was time to explain the deal and how Billy’s complicated technology worked.

但现在事情变得更加棘手了。是时候解释这笔交易以及比利那套复杂的技术是如何运作的了。

I would do this using a Pre-Wired Idea (more on that in the next chapter). But before I could get to any of that, we had to provide the bankers with a sense of complete confidence that Billy and his product were worthy of a $10 million investment, and that he would use the money properly. We had to instill certainty.

我会利用预先构思好的方案来实现这一点（下一章会详细介绍）。但在那之前，我们必须让银行家们完全相信比利和他的产品值得1000万美元的投资，并且他会妥善使用这笔钱。我们必须让他们确信无疑。

When you ask someone to give you $10 million, it’s stressful for all involved; because the stakes are high and a no really means no. You need the buyer to feel certain you’re a good bet. The problem Billy and I were about to face is that when the “buyer” is a team of the most calculating and risk-averse men on the planet, and you are an ex–Ohio State linebacker turned computer security guy from Akron, this level of certainty isn’t naturally occurring. You have to create it.

当你开口向别人要1000万美元时，对所有相关人员来说都压力巨大；因为风险很高，拒绝就意味着彻底的拒绝。你需要让买家确信你是个可靠的投资对象。我和比利即将面临的问题是，当“买家”是一群世界上最精于算计、最厌恶风险的人，而你只是个来自阿克伦、从俄亥俄州立大学橄榄球队退役后转行做计算机安全的人时，这种程度的信任并不会自然而然地产生。你必须努力去建立它。

Thankfully, my team and I have found a solution to this problem. Billy was about to fill in the Certainty Gap using a very specific type of script called a Flash Roll.

幸好，我和我的团队找到了解决这个问题的方法。比利原本打算用一种叫做“闪电滚动”的特殊脚本来填补确定性差距。

THE FLASH ROLL

闪光灯滚动

In the film My Cousin Vinny there’s a classic scene in which the prosecutor, played by Lane Smith, is questioning a witness, played by Marisa Tomei, who is on the stand as an auto mechanics expert for the defendant. Smith’s character is trying to prove that Tomei’s character, Mona Lisa Vito, an attractive Brooklynite with heavy makeup, big hair, and a too-tight dress, just isn’t qualified to comment on the details of the case. He sarcastically asks her, “Can you tell me, what would the correct ignition timing be on a 1955 Bel Air Chevrolet with a 327 cubic-inch engine and a four barrel carburetor?”

在电影《我的表兄维尼》中，有一个经典的场景：由莱恩·史密斯饰演的检察官正在质问由玛丽莎·托梅饰演的证人，这位证人是被告的汽车机械专家。史密斯饰演的角色试图证明，托梅饰演的莫娜·丽莎·维托——一位妆容浓艳、头发蓬松、穿着紧身裙的布鲁克林美女——根本没有资格对案件细节发表评论。他讽刺地问她：“你能告诉我，一辆1955年款的雪佛兰贝尔艾尔，搭载327立方英寸发动机和四腔化油器，正确的点火正时是多少吗？”

“It’s impossible to answer. . . . Nobody could answer that question!” she replies.

“这个问题无法回答……没有人能回答这个问题！”她回答道。

“Your Honor,” the lawyer declares triumphantly, “I move to disqualify Miss Vito as an expert witness.” The judge seems to agree. After all, with the hairdo and tight dress, she looks like she knows nothing about auto mechanics. Everyone in the courtroom is thinking the same thing: What could this woman possibly know about cars?

“法官阁下，”律师得意洋洋地宣布，“我提议取消维托小姐作为专家证人的资格。”法官似乎同意了。毕竟，她那发型和紧身裙，看起来对汽车机械一窍不通。法庭上的每个人都在想同一件事：这个女人能懂什么汽车？

But then Tomei delivers one of the most iconic speeches in film history. In about fifteen seconds, she establishes herself as an absolute automotive authority and completely changes the way everyone in the room sees her. Even the opposing lawyer is impressed and has nothing more to ask.

但随后，托梅发表了影史上最经典的演讲之一。短短十五秒，她就展现了自己作为汽车界权威的实力，彻底改变了在场所有人对她的看法。就连对方律师也为之折服，无话可说。

How did she instill so much certainty in so little time?

她如何在如此短的时间内给人留下如此深刻的印象？

“It is a trick question,” she said, talking a mile a minute. “’Cause Chevy didn’t make a 327 in fifty-five. The 327 didn’t come out till sixty-two. And it wasn’t offered in the Bel Air with the four-barrel carb till sixty-four. However, in 1964 the correct ignition timing would be four degrees before top dead center.”
“这是个陷阱题，”她语速飞快地说，“因为雪佛兰在1955年并没有生产327发动机。327发动机直到1962年才问世。而且直到1964年，贝尔艾尔车型才开始配备四腔化油器。不过，在1964年，正确的点火正时应该是上止点前四度。”

As she finishes, a stunned silence hangs over the courtroom. Only an expert in automotive mechanics could have made that assessment and explained it so thoroughly, rapidly, and matter-of-factly.

她讲完后，法庭上顿时一片死寂。只有汽车机械专家才能做出这样的判断，并且解释得如此透彻、迅速、客观。

“Well . . . uh . . . she’s acceptable, Your Honor,” Smith says meekly, admitting her testimony before sitting back down.

“嗯……呃……她还可以，法官大人，”史密斯怯怯地说，承认了自己的证词，然后坐了下来。

This is a classic Flash Roll —a linguistic fireworks display of pure technical mastery over a complex subject. A Flash Roll is specially designed so that no matter how skeptical your listeners are when you start talking, by the end they’ll be convinced you’re a total expert and you know your industry and your craft cold—down to the finest detail. The Flash Roll should take just sixty to ninety seconds to deliver—that’s about 250 words.

这是一段经典的“闪电演讲”（Flash Roll）——一场语言上的炫技盛宴，展现了你对复杂主题的精湛技艺。“闪电演讲”的设计理念在于，无论听众在你开始演讲时多么怀疑，到最后他们都会确信你是一位绝对的专家，对你所在的行业和你的专业领域了如指掌，甚至细致入微。一段“闪电演讲”的时长应控制在60到90秒之间——大约250个单词。

Have you ever taken a mountain bike to a repair shop? To your mind, all you know is the brakes don’t brake and the shifter doesn’t shift. The mechanic—that’s the guy with tattoos of flames, flowers, playing cards, and sea serpents covering both arms and a slick hipster mustache—takes one look and says,

你有没有把山地车送到修理店？在你看来，你只知道刹车失灵，变速器也变不了档。修车师傅——就是那个双臂纹着火焰、花朵、扑克牌和海蛇，留着时髦小胡子的家伙——看了一眼就说：

“Look here, you’ve got a worn control arm at the gear insert point where the guard plate connects to the head sprocket and you have too much plate pressure so the trailing edge is catching the gear cable every time the spoke set rotates toward the center hub . . .”
“你看这里，齿轮插入点处的控制臂磨损了，护板与头链轮连接的地方压力过大，导致护板后缘每次辐条组向中心轮毂旋转时都会卡住变速线……”

“Stop,” you say, fully convinced this hipster bike guru is a technical master in your precise problem. You happily hand over the bike and leave with complete certainty that the fix will be done correctly, to the highest standards. This makes the seven-hundred-dollar repair bill easier to swallow.

“停！”你说道，完全相信这位时髦的自行车专家是解决你遇到的问题的技术大师。你欣然将自行车交给他，离开时确信他能以最高标准把车修好。这样一来，七百美元的维修费也就更容易接受了。

Without knowing it, the bike shop repair guy just delivered an impeccable Flash Roll.

自行车店的修理工不知不觉中完成了一次完美的闪电卷。

A good Flash Roll must locate a problem; it should take a point of view and it should arrive at a deductive conclusion about how to solve a problem. You need a Flash Roll with a clear beginning, middle, and end.

好的快速思考题必须找到问题所在；它应该提出一个观点，并得出解决问题的演绎结论。你需要一个结构清晰、有始有终的快速思考题。

What you want to create is an action sequence delivered in highly technical language, which does something special to establish you as an expert. There are no creative thoughts in a Flash Roll. There are no statements such as “then I noticed” or “and that made me start to wonder” or “I started to think.” You are not explaining your philosophy, you are not throwing out a few ideas for discussion, or passionately talking about your business or product. In fact, a Flash Roll is told dispassionately, with no emotional content or display. It must be completely stripped of all editorial, emotional, and extraneous details; it is literally just a list of technical actions that can be taken to solve a very difficult problem. Without ego or pride or even charisma, it should explain exactly what you did to solve a specific problem and then what the outcome was. And, of course, what makes the Flash Roll fun to perform is that you are going to purposely use dense, technical jargon that most people will tell you to avoid using. For about ninety seconds you’re flipping the script.

你想要打造的是一段用高度专业化的语言呈现的操作流程，以此来确立你的专家地位。快速演示中不包含任何创意性的想法，也没有“然后我注意到”、“这让我开始思考”或“我开始思考”之类的语句。你无需阐述你的理念，无需抛出一些想法供人讨论，也无需热情洋溢地谈论你的业务或产品。事实上，快速演示需要保持冷静客观，不带任何情感色彩。它必须完全剥离所有编辑性、情感性和无关的细节；它仅仅是一系列可以解决棘手问题的技术操作。它应该摒弃一切自负、骄傲甚至个人魅力，准确地解释你如何解决特定问题以及最终结果。当然，快速演示的乐趣在于，你要刻意使用大多数人都会建议你避免使用的晦涩难懂的专业术语。在短短九十秒内，你将颠覆常规。

An important and helpful step is to memorize your Flash Roll so you can deliver it at double your usual rate of speech. Think of it this way: As written, a Flash Roll is about 250 words. Your average rate of speech is probably 125 words per minute. So in order to get through the whole thing in about 60 seconds you’ll need to speed things up quite a bit. When you’re delivering a well-written Flash Roll at this pace, the buyer has the sense that you’re simply describing something you’ve done or dealt with a hundred times before. Especially when you make such a decisive conclusion on such a specific problem so matter-of-factly. The fast pace with the technical detail and the finite conclusion will make it obvious that you are for real.

一个重要且有效的步骤是记住你的快速陈述，这样你就可以以两倍于平时的语速进行陈述。不妨这样想：一份完整的快速陈述大约有250个单词。你平均的语速大概是每分钟125个单词。所以，为了在60秒左右说完，你需要大幅加快语速。当你以这样的速度陈述一份条理清晰的快速陈述时，买家会觉得你只是在描述一件你已经做过或处理过无数次的事情。尤其当你对一个具体问题如此斩钉截铁地得出结论时，这种感觉会更加强烈。快速的语速、详尽的技术细节以及明确的结论，会让买家确信你是认真的。

You start a Flash Roll by making the problem seem simple, routine, or trivial—to you, it’s something so basic, you hardly think about it. For example, consider a time you showed your doctor a rash that wouldn’t go away. “Oh, yeah,” the doctor said, “we deal with these all the time. Had a guy in last week with a plant-induced contact dermatitis . . . had to give him furosemide to reduce the edema, and do a quick photorefractive laser pass; it was touch and go, but finally got him out of critical with an endovascular aortic aneurysm repair. . . . But looking at yours, it doesn’t look half as bad as that—you probably just got a meningococcal septicemia and we can knock it out with a course of epinephrine and quick Z-Pak.” And even though you don’t have a clear idea what she’s talking about, you instantly relax. Good , you think, this doctor knows what she’s doing. I’m in good hands here .

你一开始会先把问题描述得简单、常规或微不足道——对你来说，这简直是小事一桩，你根本不会去想它。比如，想想你曾经带医生去看你身上那块怎么也消不下去的皮疹。“哦，是啊，”医生说，“我们经常遇到这种情况。上周有个病人，是植物引起的接触性皮炎……我们给他用了呋塞米来消肿，还做了个快速的光折射激光治疗；情况一度很危急，但最终通过血管内主动脉瘤修复术把他救了回来……不过，你的情况看起来没那么严重——你可能只是得了脑膜炎球菌败血症，我们用肾上腺素和速效阿奇霉素就能治好。” 即使你不太明白她在说什么，你也会立刻放松下来。你心想，太好了，这位医生很专业。我在这里很放心。

To write your Flash Roll, describe the problem being faced, your assessment of it, your proposed solution, and the economics (how long it would take and what the results would be, or were the last time you did it). Freely use technical terms, writing exactly the way you talk to the technical people in your business when you’re one-on-one with them. Recall the mechanic at the bike shop explaining what is wrong with your bike. Or think about the doctor who looks at the rash and says, “It’s nummular dermatitis. We’ll treat it with oral cyclosporine, topical corticosteroids, and ultraviolet light therapy for a week and you’ll be good as new.”

撰写你的快速解决方案时，请描述你面临的问题、你对问题的评估、你提出的解决方案以及经济效益（需要多长时间，结果如何，或者你上次这样做的结果如何）。请随意使用技术术语，就像你与公司技术人员一对一交流时那样。想想自行车店的技工是如何解释你的自行车出了什么问题。或者想想医生看了你的皮疹后说：“这是钱币状皮炎。我们会用口服环孢素、外用皮质类固醇和紫外线疗法治疗一周，你就会完全康复。”

Doctors have a flawless Flash Roll. They may not call it that, but their job depends upon their ability to instill a sense of certainty in patients that the treatment they are proposing will work for them—even while at the same time saying, “There’s no guarantee this will work.”

医生们拥有完美的“闪电战”技巧。他们可能不会这样称呼它，但他们的工作取决于他们能否让患者确信他们提出的治疗方案会有效——即使与此同时他们还要说：“这并不能保证一定有效。”

The manner in which you deliver the Flash Roll is of the utmost importance. You absolutely cannot be seeking validation or opinion of the buyer. You are the expert, not he. This Flash Roll assessment shouldn’t come across as something you are proud of. Instead, it should seem like it’s mundane and uninteresting to you—not a big deal at all; like if I asked you about it in a few hours you might forget you even said it.

你呈现快速评估报告的方式至关重要。你绝对不能寻求买家的认可或意见。你是专家，不是他。这份快速评估报告不应该让你显得引以为豪。相反，它应该表现得平淡无奇，对你来说毫无意义——根本无关紧要；就好像几个小时后我问起它，你可能都忘了自己说过这件事似的。

To end the Flash Roll, you can just shrug and say something like, “Anyway, based on what I’m seeing here, that’s what I would do.” Then leave it all behind and move on to the next part of the presentation.

结束快速演示时，你可以耸耸肩说：“总之，根据我目前看到的，我会这么做。”然后把这一切都抛在脑后，继续进行演示的下一部分。

In one deal I was involved with, Heather, an incredibly smart sales executive wasn’t getting a good hit rate on her sales calls with clients. Heather was extremely knowledgeable about her industry and how her software worked, but no sales were coming in. So they sent me out to talk to her.

我参与过的一个项目中，销售主管希瑟非常聪明，但她的客户拜访成功率却很低。希瑟对她所在的行业以及她开发的软件运作方式都非常了解，但却始终没有销售业绩。所以他们派我去和她谈谈。

As soon as I saw her I immediately understood what the problem was. Heather had cropped blond hair, a rock climber’s physique, and dressed smartly for the corporate world. And she was trying to sell software to logging mills, a completely male-dominated industry where everyone drives pickup trucks, has a chainsaw or two in the back, and in October every year puts enough frozen elk in the fridge to make it through winter. Most sawmills haven’t been modernized and suffer from operating inefficiencies, which Heather’s software is designed to fix, but first she had to convince a bunch of male sawmill executives to see her as an expert, to trust her recommendations, and most important, to be certain her software would fix their problems.

我一见到她就立刻明白了问题所在。希瑟留着一头利落的金发，身材健硕，像个攀岩运动员，穿着打扮也十分干练，很符合职场人士的形象。她正在向伐木厂推销软件，这是一个完全由男性主导的行业，每个人都开着皮卡，车厢里放着一两台电锯，每年十月都会在冰箱里囤积足够过冬的冷冻麋鹿肉。大多数伐木厂都没有进行现代化改造，运营效率低下，而希瑟的软件正是为了解决这些问题而设计的。但首先，她必须说服一群男性伐木厂高管，让他们把她视为专家，相信她的建议，最重要的是，让他们确信她的软件能够解决他们的问题。

I worked with her to write a Flash Roll that would get the mills’ executives believing in her abilities, and told her to practice it as if she were learning a movie script. Yes, it was a business presentation, but I suggested she treat it more like a theatrical performance—after all, it was just for 60 seconds. At her next meeting, she landed the sale and over the years became a top executive in the industry.

我与她合作撰写了一份“闪电演讲稿”，旨在让工厂高管们相信她的能力。我告诉她要像练习电影剧本一样练习。没错，这是一份商业演讲稿，但我建议她把它当作一场戏剧表演来对待——毕竟，它只有短短的60秒。在下一次会议上，她成功拿下订单，并在之后的几年里成长为业内顶尖的高管。

Here’s what I wrote for her, word for word:

以下是我一字不差地写给她的内容：

Last week I was at one of your competitors’ sawmills, where I saw that a new 50-megawatt heat-recovery unit had been installed on a black-liquor recovery boiler with a 2,000 tons per day load setting. They spent $50,000 to install it, because of a 200-watt surge-induced power shutdown, but they didn’t think to regulate the motor brush speed, so that Johnson X4 heat-recovery unit needed to be removed and rebuilt with ceramic 228-needle bearings. The problem turned out to be a 75-psi pressure drop at the weld joint where the air fans couldn’t handle the volume, shutting down and jamming all the NOS valves. We came in to install and override the processor and adjust the electronic pressure valves, which worked perfectly first time out, and they’ve increased pump production 200 percent, saving over $10,000 a month on downtime.
上周我去了你们一家竞争对手的锯木厂，看到他们在一个日处理量为2000吨的黑液回收锅炉上安装了一台新的50兆瓦热回收装置。由于一次200瓦的浪涌电流导致的停电，他们花了5万美元安装这台装置，但他们没有想到要调节电机电刷转速，所以这台Johnson X4热回收装置不得不拆下来，用228陶瓷滚针轴承重新组装。问题出在焊接接头处75磅/平方英寸的压降，那里的风机无法承受风量，导致风机停止运转并卡住了所有的NOS阀门。我们到场后安装并接管了处理器，并调整了电子压力阀，一次就完美运行，泵的生产效率提高了200%，每月节省了超过1万美元的停机成本。

Boom. That’s only 138 words. About one minute of speaking at normal speed—thirty seconds when done at double the normal pace, as I instructed Heather to do.

好了，只有138个单词。正常语速的话大约需要一分钟——如果按照我指示希瑟的语速加倍，只需要30秒。

Even though I wrote Heather’s Flash Roll, I didn’t fully understand it. Most people would not. Arguably, the experienced sawmill executives—the very people who are supposed to understand this stuff—might not get it 100 percent. The goal, of course, is not to deliver this kind of information for understanding and comprehension, but to demonstrate your technical mastery in such a way that you create certainty about your authority and expertise.

尽管我写了《希瑟的闪光卷》，但我自己也没完全理解。大多数人可能也一样。可以说，那些经验丰富的锯木厂高管——那些理应理解这些内容的人——也未必能百分之百领会。当然，目的并非在于让别人理解和领会这类信息，而是为了展现你的技术造诣，从而确立你的权威和专业水平。

Your customers don’t actually want to know the details that are in your head. If you gave them the full benefit of your industry knowledge, it would be overwhelming and time consuming, moving further from a sale rather than closer. So you create a mini-performance that you own, by delivering the Flash Roll with focus and impact. Of course, most of us aren’t professional performers (this is why you keep a short time limit). But for this brief display of mastery of the details, we can all learn our Flash Roll script. It’s critical to do this correctly because the buyer wants the comfort and confidence that you are an absolute expert, that you can get down in the weeds if needed, and the Flash Roll shows them that you can do it, without question.

你的客户其实并不想了解你脑子里所有的细节。如果你把所有的行业知识都一股脑地倾囊相授，只会让他们感到不知所措，耗时费力，最终反而会适得其反，离成交越来越远。所以，你需要精心打造一场属于你自己的“闪回演示”，以专注而有力的方式呈现。当然，我们大多数人都不是专业表演者（这也是为什么你要控制时间的原因）。但为了这短暂而精湛的演示，我们都可以学习一下“闪回演示”的脚本。正确演绎“闪回演示”至关重要，因为买家需要的是你作为绝对专家的可靠和自信，需要你能够深入细节，而“闪回演示”恰恰能让他们确信这一点。

In Geneva, Billy was going to deliver a Flash Roll we’d spent weeks crafting. But first, he had to lay the foundation.

在日内瓦，比利要交付我们花了数周时间精心制作的闪光卷。但首先，他得先打好基础。

WARMING UP

热身

“Gentlemen,” Billy began, “the top ten highest-paying jobs in data security today are . . .”

“先生们，”比利开口说道，“目前数据安全领域收入最高的十大职位是……”

[long pause for dramatic effect]

[长时间停顿，以增强戏剧效果]

“. . . are all completely bootleg, criminal, and illegal. Let me quickly tell you about the top three moneymakers in the data industry:

“……这些都是彻头彻尾的盗版、犯罪和非法行为。让我快速介绍一下数据行业中最赚钱的三大领域：

  1. Stealing data and reselling it on the dark web
    窃取数据并在暗网上转售
  2. Encrypting data and ransoming companies to decrypt it
    对数据进行加密并勒索公司以求解密。
  3. Hacking bank accounts and making fraudulent transfers
    入侵银行账户并进行欺诈性转账

“And the good ol’ days of the amateur account hacking ransomer are gone,” Billy continued. “We now have well-funded nation-states like North Korea, and traffickers like the Sinaloa Cartel who are playing the bank fraud game.

“业余黑客入侵账户勒索赎金的美好旧时光已经一去不复返了，”比利继续说道。“现在我们有像朝鲜这样资金雄厚的国家，还有像锡那罗亚贩毒集团这样的走私贩子，他们都在玩弄银行诈骗的把戏。”

“When you hear the word hacker , what’s the first thing that comes to mind? A twentysomething alone in a basement, wearing a black hoodie, staring at a laptop. In the old days, sure, you wouldn’t be far off the mark. Now we’re defending banks from professional criminals: the Russian Business Network, the Carbanak group, Cosa Nostra, Yakuza, and the Sinaloa drug cartel. These groups are stealing data and hacking accounts at two petabytes a day, worth $10 million each, with almost zero cost. This criminal activity is invisible to most corporations, until it’s way too late, so hardly anyone will pay the high cost of installing better safety systems.”

“一提到黑客，你首先想到的是什么？一个二十出头的年轻人独自待在地下室，穿着黑色连帽衫，盯着笔记本电脑。在过去，你的想法或许还算贴切。但现在，我们是在保护银行免受职业犯罪分子的侵害：俄罗斯商业网络、卡巴纳克集团、黑手党、雅库扎以及锡那罗亚贩毒集团。这些组织每天窃取两PB的数据，入侵账户，每个账户价值1000万美元，而成本几乎为零。大多数公司对此浑然不觉，直到为时已晚，因此几乎没有人愿意支付高昂的费用来安装更完善的安全系统。”

Billy was starting the pitch by providing context for what was happening in the industry—the specific undercover forces that most people didn’t know much about—further cementing his status as a cybersecurity guru.

比利在开始推销时，首先介绍了行业内正在发生的事情——大多数人不太了解的具体幕后力量——这进一步巩固了他作为网络安全专家的地位。

“Today,” he continued, “the hackers are well-funded, difficult to identify, and very industrious because the black market for stolen data pays so well. Incredibly well, in fact. It’s a world of high revenue and high profit. A criminal data operation can go all the way from idea in the morning to operations in the afternoon and then to a million in profits by the end of the day.”

他继续说道：“如今，黑客资金雄厚，难以追踪，而且非常勤奋，因为窃取数据的黑市利润丰厚。事实上，利润高得惊人。这是一个高收入、高利润的世界。一个犯罪数据行动可以从早上构思，下午开始实施，到当天结束就能赚取百万美元的利润。”

I had written this section of the presentation around the Pre-Wired Idea “Winter Is Coming” (more on this in the next chapter), painting a picture for the bankers of just how bad things were getting. We needed to show them that the world they lived in was becoming unacceptably dangerous.

我撰写这部分演示文稿时，围绕着“凛冬将至”这个预设理念（下一章会详细介绍），力图向银行家们描绘出事态究竟恶化到了何种程度。我们需要让他们明白，他们所处的世界正变得危险到令人无法接受。

“Hackers will try all day every day to access your customers’ accounts and their money,” Billy said, staying on script. “That’s why you ask each customer so many questions when they call, right? But the customer is annoyed by so many questions—birth date, PIN, last transaction, et cetera—and the process takes an average of five minutes and seventeen seconds to ask them all the identifying questions. You and your customers currently accept this inconvenience as a necessary evil to keep the transaction ‘secure.’ But customers sure don’t like it.”

“黑客每天都会想方设法入侵您客户的账户和资金，”比利照本宣科地说。“所以您才会在客户打电话来时问他们那么多问题，对吧？但客户会被这些问题搞得心烦意乱——生日、密码、最近一次交易等等——而且问完所有身份验证问题平均要花五分十七秒。您和您的客户目前都把这种不便视为确保交易‘安全’的必要之恶。但客户肯定不喜欢这样。”

Some of the bankers were nodding their heads. This was definitely hitting home.

一些银行家频频点头。这番话确实说到他们心坎里了。

“What if it wasn’t necessary at all?” Billy continued. “Our system identifies the caller using their voice in less than five seconds. We’re never wrong. We do this using our own unique voice identification algorithm and by comparing what they are saying to every known piece of information about them on the internet. If there is any question they are not the person they say they are, we flag it immediately. It’s an unbeatable system.”

“如果根本不需要呢？”比利继续说道，“我们的系统只需不到五秒钟就能通过声音识别来电者。我们从未出错。我们使用独有的语音识别算法，并将他们所说的话与互联网上所有已知的关于他们的信息进行比对。如果对来电者的身份有任何疑问，我们会立即标记出来。这是一个无懈可击的系统。”

Because this topic was so relevant to the bankers’ world, they became visibly emotional as Billy spoke, shifting in their seats.

由于这个话题与银行家的世界息息相关，所以当比利讲话时，他们都明显情绪激动，在座位上挪动着身子。

Their body language revealed that Billy’s words were hitting a nerve. But I knew we were still a distance from the finish line. We still had to convince them that Billy was capable of spearheading this company—and was worthy of the $10 million we were asking for.

他们的肢体语言表明，比利的话触动了他们的痛处。但我知道我们离目标还很远。我们仍然需要让他们相信，比利有能力领导这家公司，并且值得我们要求的1000万美元。

As if on cue, Philippe spoke up. “Tell us, Billy, if we provide you ten million dollars, how will you spend it?”

仿佛是事先安排好的一样，菲利普开口问道：“比利，告诉我们，如果我们给你一千万美元，你会怎么花？”

Now, Billy was tech smart. He was food smart. He knew literature, seventeenth-century architecture, college football rankings, and, of course, cybersecurity. But he wasn’t the greatest at international diplomacy. He jumped in without giving a thought to where we were (Geneva), whom we were asking for money (Swiss citizens), and where we were going to haul their money off to (Akron, Ohio).

比利精通科技，也懂美食。他了解文学、十七世纪建筑、大学橄榄球排名，当然还有网络安全。但他并不擅长国际外交。他贸然行事，根本没考虑我们身在何处（日内瓦），我们向谁要钱（瑞士公民），以及我们要把钱运到哪里（俄亥俄州阿克伦）。

He rushed to explain. “First, we’re going to build a new data center in Akron. Then, we’re going to hire six or seven engineers from Carnegie Mellon University in Pittsburgh. And we’re going to partner with NASDAQ. And our first big customer is going to be Bank of America. We might park a couple employees at their headquarters in Charlotte, North Carolina . . . just for a while to monitor implementation.”

他连忙解释道：“首先，我们将在阿克伦新建一个数据中心。然后，我们将从匹兹堡的卡内基梅隆大学聘请六七名工程师。我们还将与纳斯达克合作。我们的第一个大客户将是美国银行。我们可能会安排几名员工暂时驻扎在他们位于北卡罗来纳州夏洛特的总部……以便监督实施情况。”

In other words, all that money would leave Switzerland, be spent in the United States, and only benefit Americans. He might as well have said we were going to spend it all on new outfits for the Dallas Cowboys Cheerleaders. This was not what they wanted to hear.

换句话说，所有这些钱都会离开瑞士，花在美国，最终只会让美国人受益。他这话就好像在说我们要把所有钱都用来给达拉斯牛仔啦啦队买新衣服一样。这可不是他们想听到的。

“Why should we give this American company our investment when there is no benefit to the Swiss?” Philippe asked, starting to frown. “The board of directors would never approve it.”

“瑞士人得不到任何好处，我们为什么要把投资给这家美国公司？”菲利普问道，眉头开始皱了起来。“董事会绝不会批准的。”

There was a long silence as the body language in the room cooled off. Pens were put down, chairs were pushed back, coffee cups clanged back in their saucers. It was like one of those scenes in the movies when a character does something embarrassing and the music comes to a screeching halt.

房间里一片寂静，气氛渐渐冷却下来。笔被放下，椅子被推开，咖啡杯重重地放回碟子里。这场景就像电影里某个角色做了什么尴尬的事，音乐戛然而止的画面。

We were in trouble. I jumped in.

我们遇到了麻烦。我挺身而出。

“Guys, let me interrupt here for a moment because the best part of our plan is next—we’re going to open an office in Zurich.” Their heads nodded approvingly. Whew. “And we’re going to hire five Swiss engineers and open an internship at Swiss Federal Institutes of Technology.” More vigorous nodding. “And, of course, we would expect to hold the annual board meeting right here in this office—if you would agree, of course.” A murmur of approval.

“各位，我先插播一下，因为我们计划中最精彩的部分还在后面——我们要在苏黎世开设一个办事处。”他们点头表示赞同。呼。“我们还要聘请五位瑞士工程师，并在瑞士联邦理工学院设立实习岗位。”他们更加用力地点头。“当然，如果你们同意的话，我们希望年度董事会就在这里召开。”一阵低声的赞同。

This explanation diplomatically solved the geopolitical turf war Billy had started. But now we had a new problem: Billy’s slip-up had broken the spell we’d cast over the Swiss investors. I could see them sliding back into their natural state of skepticism. They started firing questions at us.

这种解释巧妙地化解了比利挑起的地缘政治争端。但现在我们又面临一个新问题：比利的失言打破了我们之前对瑞士投资者施加的信任。我看到他们又恢复了原本的怀疑态度，开始向我们抛出一连串的问题。

“What do the projections look like?”

“预测结果如何？”

“Do you have a CFO?”

你们有首席财务官吗？

“How many additional rounds of finance will be required? Who will buy us when we want to sell the company off?”

“还需要多少轮融资？当我们想出售公司时，谁会收购我们？”

Rapid-fire, penetrating questions like this are always bad news. With his statement, Billy had unintentionally triggered the buyers’ brains to become aware of the risks we were offering. Now these bankers were trying to gather more information to close their Certainty Gap. The problem is, we didn’t want to answer, because answering technical questions is an inefficient way to close the Certainty Gap and rarely works with sophisticated buyers.

像这样连珠炮似、一针见血的问题总是坏消息。比利的话无意中让买家意识到了我们所提供产品的风险。现在，这些银行家正试图收集更多信息来弥补他们的“确定性缺口”。问题是，我们不想回答，因为回答技术性问题并不能有效地弥补“确定性缺口”，而且对那些经验丰富的买家来说，这种方法也鲜有成效。

We needed to figure out a way to flip the script. To do so, Billy had been practicing an incredible Flash Roll, and this was the right moment to launch it.

我们需要想办法扭转局面。为此，比利一直在练习一个精彩的闪电翻滚，现在正是施展它的最佳时机。

I took a gamble and interrupted the interrogation. “Gentlemen,” I said, and pointed to one of the men at the table, “one question at a time, please. Professor Schumacher, what were you asking?”

我冒险打断了审问。“先生们，”我指着桌边的一位男士说，“请一次只问一个问题。舒马赫教授，您刚才问的是什么？”

“You obviously know data security,” said Schumacher, “but our banking industry has special requirements; these may be difficult for you to learn in such a short amount of time.”

舒马赫说：“你显然了解数据安全，但我们银行业有特殊的要求；这些要求你可能很难在这么短的时间内学会。”

I breathed a silent sigh of relief. It was the perfect question to set Billy loose with the Flash Roll. Billy rushed in to answer again, but now he was in the right domain—cybersecurity, not international diplomacy; this was an area where he was an expert. And this time he was about to deliver a well-rehearsed masterpiece of a speech, not just something he was making up off the cuff.

我暗自松了口气。这个问题简直是让比利使出“闪电战”的绝佳机会。比利再次急忙作答，但这次他谈对了领域——网络安全，而不是国际外交；这才是他的专长。而且这一次，他要发表的是一篇精心准备的精彩演讲，而不是即兴发挥。

These guys were about to see a very different side of Billy Cambell.

这些人即将看到比利·坎贝尔截然不同的一面。

Billy grabbed a fat strawberry from the platter in the center of the table and dipped it into the bowl of sugar by the teapot, and the whole thing disappeared into his mouth. He was fueling up for his Flash Roll, or as I sometimes call it, a drum solo. He knew this was his chance to make the deal happen.

比利从桌子中央的盘子里抓起一颗饱满的草莓，蘸了蘸茶壶旁的糖碗，然后一口吞了下去。他这是在为他的“闪电战”（Flash Roll，或者我有时称之为“鼓独奏”）做准备。他知道这是促成交易的绝佳机会。

A $10 MILLION FLASH ROLL

耗资1000万美元的闪电卷

“Unlike retail or manufacturing,” Billy said, swallowing the strawberry in a single bite but not slurring a word, “banking IT security controls are required by the EURO2 to be multilayered and to meet Unified Threat Management on the server side, which requires firewalling, intrusion detection, and anti-malware. We use Palo Alto Networks’ PA-5000 to cover these gaps. But that leaves software-based security holes on user-side devices, so all banking exchanges will audit your end point protection to go beyond the host-based network side and do an AJAX pass root on the user’s device in external environments that do not provide network-based security controls like Level 3 Boundary Protection. Of course, we still need data encryption rest cycles at two hundred milliseconds, which goes way beyond end point encryption to wide net cloud storage. That’s why the ten largest banks are using multifactor cryptographic tokens and biometrics at the current ISO2026 standards, a system that we designed and sold them.”
“与零售或制造业不同，”比利一边说着，一边一口吞下草莓，但说话却丝毫没有含糊不清，“根据EURO2的要求，银行业IT安全控制必须是多层级的，并且需要在服务器端满足统一威胁管理（UTM）的要求，这需要防火墙、入侵检测和反恶意软件。我们使用Palo Alto Networks的PA-5000来弥补这些漏洞。但这会在用户端设备上留下基于软件的安全漏洞，因此所有银行交易所都会审核您的终端保护，以超越基于主机的网络层面，并在不提供基于网络的安全控制（例如三级边界保护）的外部环境中，对用户设备进行AJAX根路径验证。当然，我们仍然需要200毫秒的数据加密间隔，这远远超出了终端加密的范畴，并扩展到了更广泛的云存储领域。这就是为什么十大银行都在使用符合当前ISO2026标准的多因素加密令牌和生物识别技术，而这套系统正是我们设计并销售给他们的。”

Whoa. What just happened?

哇，刚才发生了什么？

This went way beyond Status Alignment and hit every credibility button a banker has.

这远远超出了地位一致性的范畴，触动了银行家所重视的每一条信誉按钮。

Although Billy was speaking in a very matter-of-fact tone, he was also speaking at twice his normal pace, so this was an impressive amount of technical detail in a short period of time. It was a drum solo of sorts, a wonderfully produced Flash Roll, for bankers only, and when he wrapped up, there was no question that Billy was a technical master in the domain of banking security—precisely the kind this bank needed to solve their problems. He didn’t tell them how he “knew” the industry or list his extensive credentials. He showed them. It was an easy script for him, scrolling through technical details, software vendors, the tech stack, and security implementation. After this speech, there was little question in anyone’s mind that Billy was the only man for the job. They thought to themselves, “He is the one to protect us all.”

尽管比利说话的语气非常平淡，但他的语速却是平时的两倍，因此在短时间内讲解了大量的技术细节，令人印象深刻。这简直就像一场精彩的鼓独奏，一段制作精良的银行家专属“闪电演讲”。演讲结束后，毫无疑问，比利是银行安全领域的专家——正是这家银行解决问题所需要的专家。他没有告诉他们自己是如何“了解”这个行业的，也没有列举自己丰富的资历。他用实际行动证明了这一点。对他来说，这简直是信手拈来，他轻松地讲解了技术细节、软件供应商、技术栈以及安全实施方案。演讲结束后，几乎所有人都确信，比利是这项工作的唯一人选。他们心想：“他就是那个能保护我们所有人的人。”

“Well, thank you, gentlemen,” said Lustenberger a few minutes later, after we’d finished the rest of our pitch and turned over full autonomy to the bankers (a technique I reveal in chapter six). “We’ll talk it over and get back to you in a couple of days. Are you in Geneva long?”

“好的，谢谢各位先生，”几分钟后，在我们完成剩余的推介并将全部自主权交给银行家之后（我将在第六章详细介绍这一技巧），卢斯滕贝格尔说道，“我们会仔细商量，过几天再回复你们。你们会在日内瓦待很久吗？”

“We’re leaving on Friday and need to know by then,” I said matter-of-factly.

“我们周五就要出发了，所以在那之前需要知道结果，”我语气平淡地说。

The very next morning we got the call from Philippe. The bank came in with the $10 million investment we’d wanted. Project Windbreak was a success. Billy’s Flash Roll had turned the tides at a critical moment and left the questioning bankers with a sense of complete certainty about our experience, depth, and know-how.

第二天一大早，我们就接到了菲利普的电话。银行兑现了我们想要的1000万美元投资。防风林项目取得了成功。比利的闪电融资在关键时刻扭转了局势，让那些质疑的银行家们对我们的经验、实力和专业知识彻底信服。

We went out in search of a restaurant to celebrate and ordered three of everything, which was just about the right amount.

我们外出寻找一家餐厅庆祝，每样菜都点了三份，份量刚刚好。

#↩

CHAPTER 4

第四章

Using Pre-Wired Ideas

利用预先设定的思路

S ince my first book came out, we’ve had to keep the office doors locked to prevent the large number of visitors, walk-ins, and occasional crazies. Accordingly, I was surprised to see an elderly rabbi shuffle into my personal office at ten o’clock on a Monday morning. His venerable beard framed two rosy cheeks, his eyes looked like windows to a different century. He was sporting a black suit and the kind of large leather briefcase my grandfather probably carried back in Lithuania.

自从我的第一本书出版以来，我们不得不把办公室的门锁上，以防止大量的访客、不速之客以及偶尔出现的怪人闯入。因此，周一早上十点，我看到一位年长的拉比蹒跚地走进我的私人办公室，着实吃了一惊。他浓密的胡须衬托着两颊红润，眼神仿佛来自另一个世纪。他身着黑色西装，手提着一个我祖父当年在立陶宛时可能用过的那种大型皮质公文包。

I stared at the rabbi and he stared back at me in an awkward silence. Then his face erupted into a huge smile as he pulled a carefully folded piece of paper out of his breast pocket and held it out to me. I did not take it.

我盯着拉比，他也回望着我，气氛尴尬地沉默着。突然，他脸上绽放出灿烂的笑容，从胸前的口袋里掏出一张叠得整整齐齐的纸，递给我。我没有接。

“You see?” he asked, as if I was supposed to know. It was a newspaper clipping announcing the launch of a biotech fund by a major venture capital firm. They had $200 million to spend and the rabbi wanted some of it.

“看到了吗？”他问，好像我应该知道似的。那是一份报纸剪报，宣布一家大型风险投资公司成立了一项生物科技基金。他们有两亿美元可以支配，而这位拉比也想分一杯羹。

“I need to meet with New Icon Capital right away,” he said, “and get twenty-two million.” He sat down across from me, opened his briefcase, and started pulling out papers slowly and methodically, stacking them on my desk.

“我需要马上和新图标资本公司见面，”他说，“拿到两千二百万美元。” 他在我对面坐下，打开公文包，开始缓慢而有条不紊地从中取出文件，并将它们堆放在我的桌子上。

Oh man. Some days all the stuff that comes my way is overwhelming. Today, this guy needs $22 million, tomorrow that guy needs $30 million, and then some other guy needs $50 million . . . there’s no end to it.

哎，真是的。有时候，各种各样的事情接踵而至，让我应接不暇。今天这个人需要2200万美元，明天那个人需要3000万美元，然后又有人需要5000万美元……没完没了。

I excused myself from the room to ask my assistant, Amy, what the rabbi was doing in my office, why he was unpacking his briefcase, and bluntly, how we were going to get him out of there as quickly as possible.

我借口离开房间，去问我的助理艾米，拉比在我的办公室里做什么，为什么他在打开他的公文包，以及直截了当地问，我们该如何尽快把他弄出去。

Amy had been through all of this before. Nobody was supposed to get through without an appointment. “He’s on your calendar,” she told me with a shrug. “You accepted the invite.”

艾米以前经历过这一切。任何人都不应该在没有预约的情况下进入。“他已经在你的日程表上了，”她耸耸肩告诉我。“你接受了邀请。”

Now, I’m not the kind of person who checks my calendar multiple times a day, but I’m sure I would have noticed “Old Guy wants $22 million” was scheduled first thing on a Monday. I checked my phone calendar and, oops, there it was: 10 a.m., Silicon Valley Mentor Program.

我不是那种一天多次查看日历的人，但我确信，如果“老头子要2200万美元”这个活动安排在周一早上，我肯定会注意到。我查看了手机日历，哎呀，果然在那儿：上午10点，硅谷导师计划。

Amy rolled her eyes. “You’re his mentor ,” she said.

艾米翻了个白眼。“你是他的导师，”她说。

I glanced back through the open door of my office at the seventy-year-old man who was still carefully withdrawing papers from his bottomless briefcase and organizing them on my desk, seemingly in his own little world. I was his mentor? No, I don’t think so.

我透过敞开的办公室门瞥了一眼，那位七十岁的老人仍在小心翼翼地从他那深不见底的公文包里取出文件，整理到我的桌子上，仿佛沉浸在自己的小世界里。我是他的导师？不，我不这么认为。

But Amy was right. A few weeks earlier, I had volunteered for an entrepreneurial mentorship organization. It’s an obligatory Silicon Valley tradition in which successful executives get paired up with a mentee who is just starting out in the business world. There are a couple of reasons why it’s nearly impossible to get out of being a mentor. Not only is it the decent thing to do (and it really doesn’t take much time), but more so, a cabal of lawyers, banks, and accounting firms basically require you to do it. The unwritten rule is, “If you want to get access to our money, you help our people out.”

但艾米说得对。几周前，我自愿加入了一个创业导师组织。这是硅谷一项不成文的传统，成功的企业高管会与一位刚刚踏入商界的创业者结对。几乎不可能拒绝担任导师，原因有二。这不仅是出于礼貌（而且真的不占用太多时间），更重要的是，一群律师、银行和会计师事务所基本上要求你必须这样做。不成文的规定是：“如果你想获得我们的资金，就得帮助我们的人。”

Here’s how these mentorship deals are supposed to work:

这些指导协议的运作方式如下：

  • You get paired up with a twentysomething wunderkind who is creating some kind of change-the-world technology.
    你会和一位二十多岁的神童搭档，他正在研发某种改变世界的技术。
  • You meet the kid for coffee, and listen intently while he shows you a demo.
    你和这个孩子约了咖啡，他给你演示的时候，你认真地听着。
  • Most of the time the demo stalls out, or shuts down, and a colorful little circle starts spinning.
    大多数情况下，演示会停滞不前或停止运行，然后一个彩色的小圆圈开始旋转。
  • You listen to a heartfelt speech about changing the world.
    你聆听了一场关于改变世界的感人演讲。
  • Next, you dutifully make some calls to get them a few investor meetings.
    接下来，你尽职尽责地打了一些电话，为他们安排了几次与投资者的会面。
  • And of course, once they meet a partner or investor, you never hear from them again. Favor done. Case closed.
    当然，一旦他们找到了合作伙伴或投资人，你就再也听不到他们的消息了。事情办妥，结案。

The entire process is supposed to take no more than a few hours, cumulatively. These mentees are supposed to be young, tech savvy, self-motivated, and . . . completely broke. You buy them a cortado and an almond croissant, listen carefully and try to help, and eventually they will go away. I was prepared for all that. But I had not signed up for this guy.

整个过程加起来应该不超过几个小时。这些学员应该年轻、精通科技、积极主动，而且……身无分文。你给他们买杯咖啡和杏仁羊角面包，认真倾听，尽力帮助他们，最后他们就会离开。我对这一切都做好了准备。但我没想到会遇到这个人。

“Look, sir,” I said, grabbing a few of his papers and stacking them back up, “I’m really not an expert in biotech. I think Simon at Biocom would be a much better fit.” The rabbi pushed one of the documents he’d laid out on my desk an inch closer to me. It was a signed contract with Humana, a $300 billion company. That got my attention. I flipped to the next document in the stack. Another signed contract with Pfizer. The next document—another contract, this one with Merck. And there was yet another one with Johnson & Johnson.

“先生，”我一边说着，一边拿起他桌上的几份文件又重新叠好，“我其实对生物科技并不精通。我觉得Biocom公司的西蒙更适合我。” 拉比把放在我桌上的一份文件往我这边推了推。那是一份和Humana公司签的合同，Humana是一家市值3000亿美元的公司。这引起了我的注意。我翻到下一份文件。又是一份和辉瑞公司签的合同。再下一份——又是一份合同，这次是和默克公司签的。还有一份是和强生公司签的。

“These are sort of interesting,” I said, quickly adding up some of the numbers in my head, while trying to remain impassive. “You have over nine hundred million dollars’ worth of contracts here.”

“这些挺有意思的，”我一边说着，一边在心里快速地算着一些数字，努力保持面无表情。“你们这里有超过九亿美元的合同。”

“One point seven billion, actually,” said the old man, blinking repeatedly. I glanced at the name on the bottom of every contract. His name was B. Rosenberg, the most rabbinical-sounding name in the world. Only this was no rabbi. Within a few seconds I had pulled up his Wikipedia page. Professor emeritus in biochemistry. Runner-up for national laureate in recombinant human genetics. Recently awarded an international fellowship in molecular medicine. An international ranking in developmental biology. And twenty other similar awards and honors, each one better than the next. It appeared that he had bounced around the early stage biotech startup world for a while, until a friend of mine, Bob Morgan at a Silicon Valley bank, decided he needed a real adviser and then referred him to me.

“确切地说是17亿，”老人眨着眼睛说道。我瞥了一眼每份合同底部的名字。他叫B·罗森伯格，这名字听起来简直像个拉比。但他可不是什么拉比。几秒钟之内，我就打开了他的维基百科页面。他是生物化学荣誉退休教授，重组人类遗传学国家奖的亚军，最近还获得了分子医学国际奖学金，在发育生物学领域也名列前茅。此外，他还获得了其他二十多项类似的奖项和荣誉，一项比一项更出色。看来他曾在早期生物技术创业公司里摸爬滚打了一段时间，直到我的朋友，硅谷一家银行的鲍勃·摩根，觉得他需要一位真正的顾问，于是把他介绍给了我。

Wow. My very first mentee had walked in with $1.7 billion in signed contracts. Maybe I should volunteer for stuff more often. After a bit more digging, I learned that a key technology he needed to satisfy these contracts, called BioSequence, was up for sale—and he was first in line to buy it. This is what he wanted the money for. The purchase would let Professor Rosenberg cash in on the contracts, establish his company as the global leader in the genetics industry, and cement his stronghold on the market for genetic tests worldwide.

哇！我的第一个学生就带着价值17亿美元的签约合同来找我。看来我应该多做些志愿工作。经过一番调查，我发现他履行这些合同所需的一项关键技术——BioSequence——正在出售，而他已经是第一个预定购买者。这就是他想要这笔钱的原因。收购BioSequence可以让罗森伯格教授从合同中获利，将他的公司打造成为全球基因检测行业的领导者，并巩固他在全球基因检测市场的统治地位。

The problem was that he was a bit difficult to understand, had a two-hour-long story about his company and its plans, and looked like he might drop dead of natural causes before these complicated plans could be put in motion. There’s no way he would be able to get the money on his own.

问题是他说话有点难懂，滔滔不绝地讲了两个小时他的公司和计划，而且看起来他随时都可能在这些复杂的计划实施之前就猝死。他根本不可能靠自己筹到这笔钱。

“Oren, you will get this meeting for me that I need very much?” he asked me then, and looked up at me with ancient, wise eyes. I wanted to help Methuselah here, but I had to level with the guy.

“奥伦，你能帮我安排一下这个我非常需要的会面吗？”他问我，抬起头，用那双古老而睿智的眼睛看着我。我本想帮帮这位老古董，但我必须跟他坦诚相待。

“There’s no way New Icon is going to give you twenty-two million dollars to buy BioSequence,” I said. His brow furrowed and his eyes filled with the sadness of the ages, and he blinked a lot. “But,” I said, and he immediately brightened. “I’m willing to bet they’ll give it to me.” He looked relieved, but you could see his mind working, like a mental abacus adding up my piece of the action, and finally realizing that I would be very expensive. But he understood that without my help the New Icon money was out of reach. And he also knew that without the New Icon money and the BioSequence technology, those fancy contracts of his were almost worthless.

“新图标公司绝对不会给你两千二百万美元买下BioSequence，”我说。他眉头紧锁，眼神里充满了岁月沉淀的悲伤，眨了好几下眼睛。“但是，”我说，他立刻精神一振。“我敢打赌他们会给我。”他看起来松了口气，但你能看出他脑子里飞速运转，就像一台心算盘在计算我能从中分一杯羹，最终意识到我身价不菲。但他明白，没有我的帮助，新图标公司的钱对他来说遥不可及。他也知道，如果没有新图标公司的资金和BioSequence的技术，他那些漂亮的合同几乎毫无价值。

Two weeks later, Professor Rosenberg and I were walking up the steps to the inner sanctum of New Icon Capital Partners in downtown San Francisco. I’d managed to get a meeting through a contact named Jim, whom I’d worked with on another deal a few years back. This wasn’t an easy meeting to get, for anybody. Their schedule was packed and my request had come out of nowhere.

两周后，我和罗森伯格教授一起走上位于旧金山市中心的新图标资本合伙公司（New Icon Capital Partners）的台阶，进入了公司的核心办公区域。这次会面是我通过一位名叫吉姆的朋友安排的，几年前我曾和他合作过另一个项目。对任何人来说，这次会面都不容易约到。他们的日程安排得满满当当，而我的请求又显得有些突兀。

“Better late than never,” Jim said, sticking his large hand out and welcoming us into New Icon’s massive reception area. Huh? I glanced at my watch. We were early, not late. This was exactly the kind of upside-down thinking you have to deal with in Silicon Valley where early was late, small was big, money was everywhere, yet impossible to get when you needed it most.

“迟到总比不到好，”吉姆说着，伸出他那只大手，热情地欢迎我们走进新偶像公司宽敞的接待区。嗯？我看了看表。我们来得早，没迟到。这就是硅谷那种颠倒的思维模式：早反而是晚，小反而是大，钱到处都是，但当你最需要的时候，却怎么也拿不到。

Jim had an imposing frame, a strong, confident face, and a crushing handshake. You knew from the second you met him he was in charge of things. Yet, he also seemed fair, a man who didn’t price his words cheaply. He was impressive. And I was impressed he’d gotten us the meeting so fast. I owed him big-time.

吉姆身材魁梧，面容坚毅自信，握手有力。你一见到他就知道他掌控着一切。然而，他也很公正，说话从不轻言。他令人印象深刻。我尤其佩服他能这么快就帮我们安排好会面。我欠他一个人情。

“Let’s head to the conference room. The investment committee is ready for your presentation now.” Jim punched a keypad and a glass door whooshed open. Jim stepped through quickly, leaving Professor Rosenberg and me scrambling to keep up. The door slid shut automatically behind us, and just like that, the two of us were inside the top-secret world of New Icon.

“咱们去会议室吧。投资委员会已经准备好听你汇报了。”吉姆按了一下键盘，玻璃门嗖地一声打开了。吉姆快步走了进去，我和罗森伯格教授手忙脚乱地跟在他身后。门在我们身后自动关上，就这样，我们俩就进入了新偶像公司这个高度机密的世界。

We followed Jim through what seemed like a mile of glass hallway. Every entrepreneur dreams of passing through these hallowed halls, because a “yes” from New Icon means your chances of becoming a huge business success go up a hundredfold. We were walking into the financial stratosphere, our footsteps echoing down the same corridors that had greeted the founders of companies such as Pandora, Ancestry.com, Casper mattresses, Postmates, Bleacher Report—all of whom were now billionaires. We walked past two thirtysomethings whom I recognized from the front pages of the Wall Street Journal , each worth over $1 billion. Today we were in the midst of people who could buy the New York Jets with a check.

我们跟着吉姆走过一条仿佛长达一英里的玻璃走廊。每个创业者都梦想着能踏入这神圣的殿堂，因为如果新偶像公司给了我们一个“同意”，那么你获得巨大商业成功的几率就会成百倍增长。我们仿佛走进了金融的巅峰，我们的脚步声回荡在同样的走廊里，这些走廊也曾迎接过潘多拉、Ancestry.com、Casper床垫、Postmates、Bleacher Report等公司的创始人——他们如今都已是亿万富翁。我们路过两位三十多岁的人，我认出他们曾出现在《华尔街日报》的头版，他们每个人的身价都超过10亿美元。今天，我们身处一群可以用支票买下整个纽约喷气机队的富豪之中。

There was no question about it. This was serious business, and I’d spent the past two weeks translating Professor Rosenberg’s obtuse and highly technical presentation into a simple pitch that investors would quickly understand and buy into. I glanced at Rosenberg, who seemed overwhelmed by the spectacle around him. I had to admit, I was feeling the pressure myself. After all, the stakes couldn’t possibly have been higher. It would be known around the world in an instant if New Icon said no. Our deal would become too toxic to touch. Today, right now, we had one bite at the apple, with no second chances. I needed to calm him down—he looked so nervous he might pass out or drop dead before we got our shot at the $22 million.

毫无疑问，这是件大事。过去两周，我一直在努力把罗森伯格教授晦涩难懂、技术性极强的演讲稿转化成投资者能够快速理解并接受的简明方案。我瞥了一眼罗森伯格，他似乎被周围的景象弄得不知所措。我不得不承认，我自己也感到压力巨大。毕竟，这事关重大。如果新偶像公司拒绝，消息会瞬间传遍全球。我们的交易将变得一文不值。今天，就在此刻，我们只有一次机会，没有第二次机会。我得让他冷静下来——他看起来紧张得好像在我们拿到那2200万美元之前就会晕倒或者猝死。

“Sometimes the best thing you can do is not think, not create scenarios, not imagine, not obsess. Just breathe, and have faith that everything will work out for the best,” I whispered to him. “If anything goes wrong, think, ‘We’ll turn lemons into lemonade.’”

“有时候，最好的办法就是什么都别想，别去设想各种情景，别去想象，别去纠结。只要深呼吸，相信一切都会好起来的，”我轻声对他说。“如果真出了什么差错，就想着，‘我们会把柠檬变成柠檬汁。’”

“Lemons?” said Jim, who did not miss a trick. “Life has never given me lemons. It has given me stress issues, a love of alcohol and a short temper,” he said, and for some reason he looked right at me.

“柠檬？”吉姆问道，他可真是个老油条。“生活从来没给我柠檬，它给了我压力、酗酒和暴躁的脾气。”他说着，不知为何直直地看着我。

“That didn’t help,” I told him, pointing at Rosenberg.

“那并没有起到作用，”我指着罗森伯格告诉他。

We heard only the echo of our own footsteps as we passed through on our way to the executive floor. The glass hallways gave way to a maze of cubicles. Everywhere we looked, young men and women were trading stocks across five time zones, looking for the slightest edge and chance to make a few million. We heard four loud beeps and a ding that meant $10 million had been scored with one tap of the enter key. Along the way we were joined by an analyst who asked if I’d like a cup of Panda Dung Tea, which, he informed me, is an artisanal brew cultivated in the mountains of Ya’an, Sichuan, and fertilized with the excrement of wild pandas under the direction of celibate monks and sells for twenty thousand dollars per pound.

我们穿过玻璃走廊，前往高管层，耳边只有自己脚步的回声。走廊尽头是迷宫般的隔间。放眼望去，到处都是年轻的男男女女，他们跨越五个时区进行股票交易，寻找着哪怕一丝一毫的优势，希望能赚到几百万美元。我们听到四声响亮的哔哔声和一声叮咚，这意味着有人一键回车，就赚到了1000万美元。途中，一位分析师走过来问我是否想喝一杯熊猫粪茶。他告诉我，这是一种产自四川雅安山区的手工茶，用野生熊猫的粪便施肥，由苦行僧指导种植，每磅售价高达两万美元。

“Can I get a Diet Coke?” I asked. “How much for a pound of that?”

“能给我来杯健怡可乐吗？”我问。“一磅多少钱？”

“That’s a Nicholas Schöffer,” the analyst told me helpfully a moment later, gesturing toward a sculpture at the base of the stairs. It was a simple hunk of half-polished metal that cost twenty trillion dollars. “Did you happen to see the unveiling of his Chronos XVII?” he continued. “Massive steel, forty-nine light projectors, and sixty-five movable disks that create a ballet of light and movement. It was a true happening.”

“那是尼古拉斯·舍费尔的作品，”分析师片刻后好心地告诉我，同时指向楼梯底部的一座雕塑。那是一块简单的半抛光金属块，价值两千亿美元。“你有没有看过他那件名为《克洛诺斯十七号》的揭幕仪式？”他继续说道，“巨大的钢铁结构，49个灯光投影仪，以及65个可移动的圆盘，共同营造出光影交错的芭蕾。那真是一场盛事。”

“Wow, no, I was out of town, but I was super bummed to miss it,” I said.

“哇，不，我当时不在城里，但我很遗憾错过了，”我说。

No question about it, this place was a money circus . . . and we were about to meet the ring master, the legendary Grant Goodman. Grant and his partner, Ross Fogelsong, founded New Icon Capital Partners back in 1989 with a small bit of money they’d pooled together. The two of them proceeded to make one savvy deal after the next, steadily growing in size and influence. Today, they run one of the most successful and widely respected venture capital funds in Silicon Valley.

毫无疑问，这里简直就是个金钱的马戏团……而我们即将见到的是这场盛宴的幕后主使，传奇人物格兰特·古德曼。格兰特和他的合伙人罗斯·福格尔松于1989年创立了新偶像资本合伙公司，当时他们只是拿出了一小笔钱。之后，两人接连做出精明的投资，公司规模和影响力稳步增长。如今，他们掌管着硅谷最成功、最受尊敬的风险投资基金之一。

“Grant’s in no mood for your personal brand of humor today, Oren,” Jim said to me under his breath as we neared the entrance to the central conference room. “Just stay on topic, and stick to the plan.” I punched Jim lightly on the shoulder, as if to say, “No worries, bud, I got this.”

“格兰特今天没心情听你那套幽默，奥伦，”吉姆在我们走向中央会议室入口时低声对我说，“别跑题，按计划来。”我轻轻捶了捶吉姆的肩膀，仿佛在说：“别担心，伙计，交给我吧。”

“Don’t do that,” Jim said.

“别那样做，”吉姆说。

A moment later, he ushered us into a large room with a perfect view of the San Francisco Bay.

片刻之后，他领我们走进一个大房间，从那里可以完美地欣赏到旧金山湾的景色。

In the center of the room was a large table. It was the size of a dozen air hockey tables. Professor Rosenberg and I found ourselves seated in front of about fifteen New Icon analysts grouped around the far end of the table. They were mostly young men wearing khakis and t-shirts with logos from companies I’d never heard of, like switcheroo.net. I did the math and realized this one-hour meeting represented some very expensive man-hours for the firm. “Good,” I thought. “They may not know who we are, but at least they’re taking the deal seriously.”

房间中央摆着一张大桌子，足足有十二张气垫球桌那么大。我和罗森伯格教授坐在桌子对面，桌子远端围着大约十五位新图标公司的分析师。他们大多是穿着卡其裤和T恤的年轻人，T恤上的公司标志我闻所未闻，比如switcheroo.net。我算了一下，意识到这一个小时的会议对公司来说耗费了不少人力。“不错，”我想，“他们可能不知道我们是谁，但至少他们对这笔交易很认真。”

The New Icon team is the best and the brightest that the entire world has to offer. These guys are valedictorians all the way back to kindergarten, with master’s degrees in computer science from Stanford, MBAs from Harvard, and for fun, a BA in ancient Greek philosophy from Yale. And most of them had not yet broken thirty.

New Icon团队汇聚了全世界最优秀、最聪明的人才。他们个个都是从幼儿园起就成绩优异的佼佼者，拥有斯坦福大学计算机科学硕士学位、哈佛大学工商管理硕士学位，甚至还有人出于兴趣，获得了耶鲁大学古希腊哲学学士学位。而且他们中的大多数人都不到三十岁。

“Here is Oren Klaff to tell you more about Gennacode,” I heard a voice saying. Someone was dimming the lights and all eyes were turning toward me. It was time to say something that would help these analysts immediately understand our company’s complex technology, business model, revenue projections, and deal terms. And I’d need to instill complete confidence that Professor Rosenberg could be trusted to lead the company.

“这位是奥伦·克拉夫，他将为大家详细介绍Gennacode公司，”我听到一个声音说道。有人调暗了灯光，所有人的目光都转向了我。我必须尽快说明情况，让这些分析师能够立刻理解我们公司复杂的技术、商业模式、收入预测和交易条款。而且，我还需要让他们完全相信，罗森伯格教授完全有能力领导这家公司。

Most people at this juncture would begin to list the specific features and benefits of their technology, giving these analysts all kinds of fodder to tear apart. After all, that’s what they’re here for. Tearing you apart is how they earn the salaries that pay for the houses and the cars and the vacations in Cap d’Antibes. I looked briefly at Grant Goodman at the far end of the table. He didn’t look too happy. What could I say to change the look of mild displeasure on the wizard’s face?

大多数人到了这个时候都会开始列举自己技术的具体特性和优势，这无疑会给分析师们提供各种各样的攻击素材。毕竟，这就是他们的工作。他们靠着把你批得体无完肤来赚取高薪，而这些薪水又能让他们买得起房子、车子，还能去昂蒂布角度假。我瞥了一眼坐在桌子另一头的格兰特·古德曼。他看起来不太高兴。我该说些什么才能让这位“专家”脸上那淡淡的不满消散呢？

It was time to hit his idea receptors and begin the process of Inception.

是时候激发他的灵感，开始“盗梦空间”的进程了。

HOW IDEA RECEPTORS WORK

思想接收器的工作原理

Information doesn’t just magically move from the outside world into your brain. There’s a process. You have to perceive something in your environment using your senses (input the data), make sense of what you are seeing (process the data), and remember it for later (save the data). And before your brain can perceive, process, and save a new idea, you need to have the right type of idea receptor waiting for that information, or it will pass through you, completely unremembered or quickly disregarded.

信息不会凭空从外部世界进入你的大脑。这需要一个过程。你必须运用感官感知环境中的事物（输入数据），理解你所看到的内容（处理数据），并将其记住以备后用（保存数据）。而且，在你的大脑能够感知、处理和保存新信息之前，你需要有合适的信息接收器来接收这些信息，否则这些信息就会从你脑海中流过，要么完全没有被记住，要么很快就被你忽略。

For instance, Nikon makes a camera called the D5. Don’t be scared by the $10,000 price tag. This thing is a bargain when you consider its incredible features. The D5 is equipped with an impressive native ISO sensitivity of 30,000, an insane 400,000-cycle shutter durability, and a drool-inducing lossless 14-bit buffering capacity of 12 images per second.

例如，尼康生产一款名为D5的相机。别被它1万美元的售价吓到。考虑到它令人惊叹的功能，这绝对是物超所值。D5拥有高达30000的原生ISO感光度、惊人的40万次快门寿命，以及令人垂涎的每秒12张无损14位缓存拍摄能力。

Woah. If those stats don’t leave you dying to get your hands on one of these then nothing will . . . and that’s exactly why Nikon doesn’t market this camera to you. If these numbers don’t already mean something to you, then it’ll be hard to sell you a D5. In fact, in order to sell you one, Nikon has to give you weeks of photography training first—just to get you to a level where you could even understand why these features are good. This isn’t just a photography problem, it’s a biology problem: If your brain doesn’t have receptors for information, the information is meaningless.

哇哦。如果这些数据都不能让你心动，那就真的没什么能让你动心了……而这正是尼康不把这款相机卖给你的原因。如果这些数据对你来说毫无意义，那么尼康就很难把D5卖给你。事实上，为了卖给你一台，尼康必须先给你提供几周的摄影培训——仅仅是为了让你达到能够理解这些功能优势的水平。这不仅仅是摄影的问题，更是生物学的问题：如果你的大脑没有接收信息的受体，那么这些信息对你来说就毫无意义。

Where do idea receptors come from? They must be built, and this process takes time. The receptors needed to fully understand the technical components of the D5 can take years of photography training to build. Nikon’s solution? Don’t even attempt to market the D5 to anybody who doesn’t already have the necessary idea receptors. Only sell these cameras to professional photographers. It’s just too difficult to talk to regular consumers about this product. But what if you specifically need to influence somebody who doesn’t already have receptors for the ideas you want to communicate? How do you start from scratch and get someone quickly up to speed on a technical, complex, or obscure big idea?

观念接受者从何而来？他们需要培养，而这个过程需要时间。要完全理解D5的技术构成，可能需要多年的摄影训练才能培养出相应的观念接受者。尼康的解决方案是什么？根本不要尝试向任何不具备必要观念接受者的人推销D5。只把这些相机卖给专业摄影师。跟普通消费者谈论这款产品实在太难了。但是，如果你需要影响那些对你想传达的观念不具备观念接受者的人呢？你如何从零开始，让他们快速掌握一个技术性强、复杂或晦涩难懂的大概念？

I’ve found there are a few receptors that everyone has that you can use when you don’t have the time to construct your own unique receptor. These receptors come pre-wired in humans at birth. Think about slot machines, drugs, alcohol, pornography, and refined sugar. These things take advantage of preexisting brain circuitry and easily activate it. The same is true of a gripping movie, a book you can’t put down, a reality show, or neighborhood gossip. The reason we feel instantly hooked is because these things take advantage of pre-wired idea receptors that exist in all our brains.

我发现每个人都拥有一些现成的受体，当你没时间构建自己独特的受体时，就可以利用这些受体。这些受体在人类出生时就已经预先设定好了。想想老虎机、毒品、酒精、色情作品和精制糖。这些东西利用了大脑中预先存在的神经回路，并能轻易地激活它们。引人入胜的电影、让你爱不释手的书、真人秀节目或邻里间的八卦也是如此。我们之所以会立刻沉迷其中，是因为这些东西利用了我们大脑中预先存在的、与生俱来的“想法受体”。

A few years back I went on a quest to identify the deepest, strongest, most ancient idea receptor in the human brain, the “godfather” of all attention-grabbing ideas. I called up a few prominent psychologists at various universities and asked them where I should start looking for this receptor and their response was surprisingly unanimous. They said I should look at three areas, in order of importance: threats, rewards, and fairness.

几年前，我开始探索人类大脑中最深层、最强大、最古老的观念受体，也就是所有吸引注意力的观念的“教父”。我联系了几所大学的知名心理学家，询问他们应该从哪里入手寻找这个受体，他们的回答出乎意料地一致。他们建议我按重要性从三个方面入手：威胁、奖励和公平。

Over the course of thousands of generations, evolution has taught us to be constantly on the lookout for these specific types of information:

经过数千代的进化，我们学会了时刻关注这些特定类型的信息：

  • APPROACHING DOOM: We automatically pay close attention to weather patterns, food shortages, political unrest, new types of weapons technology, and never-before-seen predators. It’s absolutely critical to be the first to know about any kind of doomsday scenario. This is different from the threat of a lion leaping out of the bush (a deadly threat that only has one response: Run!). Approaching doom refers to a threat that can take you by surprise because it moves too slowly for you to see it coming. A stock market crash. A new kind of predator. A food shortage.
