The first person to detect slow-moving but catastrophic threats survives; others suffer and die. The rule? Humans always give their attention to new information about big environmental threats.
    末日将至：我们本能地密切关注天气模式、粮食短缺、政治动荡、新型武器技术以及前所未见的掠食者。第一时间了解任何末日情景都至关重要。这与狮子从灌木丛中跃出的威胁（一种致命威胁，应对之策只有一条：快跑！）截然不同。末日将至指的是那些缓慢发展、难以察觉的威胁，例如股市崩盘、新型掠食者或粮食短缺。第一个发现这些缓慢但灾难性威胁的人才能幸存；其他人则会遭受苦难甚至死亡。规律是：人类总是会关注有关重大环境威胁的新信息。
  • A BIG PAYOFF: We are always looking for a huge reward for just a small amount of effort. Since there are always costs for adopting any kind of new behavior, we aren’t going to switch to something new for a puny little reward. A reward has to be nice and big before it’s really worth it for us to switch to a new behavior. The rule? Humans move quickly toward large paydays that are easy to measure and value.
    丰厚的回报：我们总是希望付出少量努力就能获得巨大的回报。然而，任何新行为的养成都需要付出代价，因此我们不会为了微不足道的回报而改变自己。只有当回报足够丰厚时，我们才会真正愿意改变自己的行为。规律是：人类会迅速追逐那些易于衡量和评估的高回报。
  • A FAIR DEAL: No one likes to be taken advantage of, and we are very sensitive to integrity, equality, and fairness. To make any kind of deal work, both parties should feel like they’re getting a fair shake. The rule? Other humans always want to be sure you have skin in the game and offer a fair deal before they say yes.
    公平交易：没有人喜欢被占便宜，我们对诚信、平等和公平都非常重视。任何交易要想成功，双方都应该感到自己得到了公平的对待。原则是：别人在答应之前，总是希望确保你投入了真金白银，并且提供的是公平的交易。

Our receptors for these specific pieces of information are so deeply wired that we cannot evaluate details of an offer until the “big three” are satisfied. Buyers, therefore, come with the desire to know three things above all else:

我们对这些特定信息的感知机制根深蒂固，以至于在“三大要素”得到满足之前，我们无法评估报价的细节。因此，买家最想知道的莫过于以下三件事：

  • WHY SHOULD I CARE? (What new threats and dangers are out there?)
    我为什么要关心？（又出现了哪些新的威胁和危险？）
  • WHAT’S IN IT FOR ME? (How can I get a better-than-average reward?)
    我能从中获得什么好处？（我怎样才能获得高于平均水平的回报？）
  • WHY YOU? (How can I trust you to give me a fair deal?)
    为什么是你？（我怎么能相信你会给我公平的交易？）

Once you answer these questions satisfactorily, the buyer will feel like they completely “get” your deal. Only then should you provide them the detailed information you have prepared. Most presentations either don’t ever answer these questions satisfactorily or they take way too long to get there. My team and I have found a simple way to get your buyers feeling like they fully understand the answers to these questions for your deal in just a few seconds. The key is to specially format the information you have so that it fits perfectly into these pre-wired idea receptors—like a key fitting a lock.

一旦您圆满解答了这些问题，买家就会感觉他们完全“理解”了您的交易。只有这样，您才能向他们提供您准备的详细信息。大多数演示要么根本无法圆满解答这些问题，要么耗时过长。我和我的团队找到了一种简单的方法，只需几秒钟就能让买家感觉他们完全理解了您交易中这些问题的答案。关键在于，要以特殊的方式组织您的信息，使其完美契合买家预先设定的认知框架——就像钥匙开锁一样。

That’s exactly what I was about to do in the New Icon conference room, standing in front of Grant Goodman and his crack team of boy geniuses. But first, I needed to align my status with theirs.

这正是我在新偶像公司的会议室里即将要做的事，当时我正站在格兰特·古德曼和他那群才华横溢的少年天才面前。但首先，我需要让自己的地位与他们保持一致。

PITCHING NEW ICON CAPITAL PARTNERS

向新任 ICON 资本合作伙伴推介

The first problem Professor Rosenberg and I faced in that room should be obvious by this point in the book. Most of the people in the room were forced to be here; this was not elective. There was some mild interest in our topic, but we were nobodies and easily forgettable. And Rosenberg was wearing a black suit, which nobody had seen outside a Bogart film retrospective in fifty years.

读到这里，罗森伯格教授和我在那间房间里遇到的第一个问题应该已经很明显了。房间里的大多数人都是被迫来的，这不是自愿参加的。虽然有人对我们的话题略感兴趣，但我们只是无名小卒，很容易被人遗忘。而且罗森伯格还穿着一套黑色西装，这种西装五十年来除了在博加特电影回顾展上，几乎没人见过。

The problem: We were going to have to quickly align ourselves or we’d strike out before we ever got up to bat. The fifteen analysts in the room had seen hundreds of ideas come and go along the way. It was their job to rip apart the plan, find the flaws, and in the process kill the deal. I had to become one of them, an insider, not an outside entrepreneur trying to finesse my way in.

问题在于：我们必须迅速调整方向，否则还没开始就已经被淘汰了。房间里的十五位分析师见证了数百个方案的兴衰更替。他们的职责就是剖析计划，找出漏洞，并最终扼杀这笔交易。我必须成为他们中的一员，成为内部人士，而不是试图钻空子、耍花招的外部创业者。

Consider for a moment what analysts are used to seeing:

不妨想想分析师们通常看到的情况：

  • A charismatic and bold entrepreneur with a great story to tell
    一位魅力四射、胆识过人的企业家，他有着一个精彩的故事要讲。
  • Colorful charts and graphs that all point one way: upward
    五彩缤纷的图表和图形，无一例外都指向同一个方向：向上
  • Dramatic data projections to show how one plus one is actually three
    引人注目的数据预测表明，一加一实际上等于三。
  • Reassurance that all these stratospheric projections are actually “conservative” and “easy to achieve”
    令人欣慰的是，所有这些高空飞行预测实际上都是“保守的”且“容易实现的”。

That’s how an entrepreneur would pitch the deal. But I wasn’t pitching to a room full of entrepreneurs. I was pitching to a room full of analysts. I needed to present the deal exactly the way an analyst would. I needed to use their own insider language to tip them off to my status as one of them and as a reliable source of information.

企业家通常会那样推销项目。但我面对的不是一群企业家，而是一群分析师。我必须像分析师那样介绍项目，使用他们内部的语言，让他们意识到我也是业内人士，而且是可靠的信息来源。

But how would an analyst present a deal to another analyst? Well, let’s start with what I know he wouldn’t do.

但是，一位分析师会如何向另一位分析师介绍一笔交易呢？好吧，我们先从我知道他不会做的事情说起。

  • He wouldn’t tell them it was a “really good deal”; instead he would show them. He would demonstrate the truth of his statements.
    他不会告诉他们这是“一笔非常划算的交易”，而是会用行动证明给他们看。他会用事实证明他所说的话是正确的。
  • He wouldn’t present a bunch of complicated facts; instead he would summarize it all in fewer than a hundred words.
    他不会罗列一大堆复杂的事实；相反，他会用不到一百个字概括所有内容。
  • He wouldn’t waste time saying please, thank you, and “How about this weather”; he would make use of every available second.
    他不会浪费时间说“请”、“谢谢”和“这天气怎么样”；他会利用每一秒时间。

An analyst talking to another analyst wouldn’t be in selling mode at all. He would strip out all the fluff, emotion, and optimism, leaving only the cold hard facts. He would show, not tell, and that’s exactly how I prepared our Status Tip-Off. But first, a critical statement to signal that I was their equal.

分析师之间交流时，绝不会抱着推销的心态。他会剔除所有无关紧要的言辞、情绪和乐观情绪，只留下冷冰冰的事实。他会用事实说话，而不是空谈，而这正是我准备“状态预警”的方式。但首先，我要发表一个关键声明，表明我与他们地位相当。

“Good morning, gentlemen, glad we could find time to get on each other’s calendars. There’s a lot to get to today, and not much time to do it, so let’s begin.” This was an introduction showing that we were equal in status to the investors—that our time was as valuable as theirs, and we weren’t there groveling for a deal. “I’ll get to the story of Gennacode and our financial plan in a moment,” I began. “But first let me catch you up to the present moment: First, I have already put our financial model through the Dodd-Frank Part 325 Stress Test [this is the equivalent of running over a child’s toy fifty times with a truck to make sure it’s safe to play with]. So, the 12-month revenue projections will show actual cash-to-be-collected. At the same time, I have also assumed that we hire 50 percent slower than our competition. And I’ve assumed we will have to pay 25 percent more for everything than they do. And I’ve assumed our customers will stretch their dollars and pay slow.”

“各位先生，早上好。很高兴我们能抽出时间互相确认一下时间。今天有很多事情要处理，但时间有限，所以我们这就开始吧。” 这段开场白表明我们与投资者地位平等——我们的时间与他们的时间一样宝贵，我们并非来此卑躬屈膝地寻求合作。“稍后我会详细介绍Gennacode公司以及我们的财务计划。”我说道。 “但首先让我简单介绍一下现状：首先，我已经按照《多德-弗兰克法案》第325部分的要求对我们的财务模型进行了压力测试（这相当于用卡车碾压儿童玩具50次，以确保其安全性）。因此，未来12个月的收入预测将显示实际可收回的现金。同时，我还假设我们的招聘速度比竞争对手慢50%。我还假设我们所有成本都将比他们高出25%。此外，我还假设我们的客户会精打细算，付款速度也会比较慢。”

With these five simple statements, only about ninety words, I’d shown that we were already looking at our plan more negatively than they ever would. In other words, I had already done their work for them, ripping the numbers apart with a “stress test” and making worst-case assumptions. This negativity is not how you would talk to an entrepreneur or a marketing guy, but it’s certainly how an analyst would talk to another analyst.

仅仅五句话，九十个字左右，我就表明我们对这个计划的审视态度比他们要消极得多。换句话说，我已经替他们完成了工作，用“压力测试”彻底剖析了数据，并做出了最坏情况的假设。这种消极的态度你不会用在和创业者或市场营销人员交谈上，但分析师之间肯定会用这种方式交流。

And it worked. Instead of rolling their eyes and starting to destroy our numbers, the New Icon analysts were leaning in, wanting to see what was behind a company that had volunteered to beat themselves up, to stress-test their own numbers with the Part 325 test, the most dreaded financial test out there. It was all very intriguing.

而且这招奏效了。新图标的分析师们并没有翻白眼、开始贬低我们的数据，而是认真倾听，想要了解这家公司背后的原因——这家公司竟然主动要求自己进行压力测试，接受最令人闻风丧胆的325部分测试。这一切都非常耐人寻味。

The classic signs of Status Alignment were now visible: more attentive body language, surprised and intrigued facial expressions, nodding of heads, setting aside of phones, dilated pupils, parted lips with hints of smiles. Next, it was time for a Flash Roll to show them we were clearheaded and well organized and could discuss critical technical details in a matter of seconds—unlike most presenters, who take hours to do this.

典型的地位认同信号此时显现：更加专注的肢体语言、惊讶和好奇的表情、点头、放下手机、瞳孔放大、嘴唇微张带着一丝微笑。接下来，我们进行了一次快速演示，向他们展示我们头脑清晰、组织有序，能够在几秒钟内讨论关键的技术细节——这与大多数演讲者需要花费数小时才能完成的工作截然不同。

“Today in the U.S.,” I continued, “health care is thirty percent of our eighteen-trillion-dollar economy. That’s pills, potions, services, surgery, and chronic care. It’s a massive market. Yet today only one technology is making high-margin dollars in every health-care segment: genetic testing. A genetic test is valuable to all consumers for three reasons: First, it predicts your chance of getting a serious disease, including cancer, diabetes, or heart disease. Second, it shows you how to prevent a disease that you are at risk of getting. Third, it will help you treat any disease condition you may already have. But how?

“如今在美国，”我继续说道，“医疗保健占我们18万亿美元经济总量的30%。这包括药品、药剂、服务、手术和慢性病护理。这是一个庞大的市场。然而，目前只有一项技术在所有医疗保健领域都能带来高额利润：基因检测。基因检测对所有消费者都有价值，原因有三：首先，它可以预测你患上严重疾病的几率，包括癌症、糖尿病或心脏病。其次，它可以告诉你如何预防你面临的疾病风险。第三，它可以帮助你治疗你可能已经患有的任何疾病。但是，它是如何做到的呢？”

“A genetic test panel will indicate how you metabolize certain drugs, which is critical information for treating any disease or adverse health condition. Today, doctors and patients need genetic tests to make any major health-care decision. We sell a genetic test panel to large corporations and governments for about $450. Why is price point important? Because it’s ten times less than anyone else. We have cut the price of genetic tests by 90 percent. What is our test specifically? A test that measures your DNA for 22,000 genetic markers, giving you information on more than 200 disease risks, drug responses, and health conditions. And the accuracy we’re getting is in the .001 error range, using high-density micro-arrays, thermo compacting nano-robotics, and the new generation of pooled-library multiplexing with a proprietary enzyme-catalyzed DNA polymerization process.”

“基因检测可以显示您如何代谢某些药物，这对于治疗任何疾病或不良健康状况都至关重要。如今，医生和患者都需要基因检测来做出任何重大的医疗保健决策。我们以大约 450 美元的价格向大型企业和政府出售基因检测套餐。价格为何如此重要？因为我们的价格比其他任何公司都低十倍。我们已将基因检测的价格降低了 90%。我们的检测具体是什么？这项检测可以测量您 DNA 中 22,000 个基因标记，从而提供超过 200 种疾病风险、药物反应和健康状况的信息。我们采用高密度微阵列、热压纳米机器人技术以及新一代混合文库多重检测技术，并结合专有的酶催化 DNA 聚合工艺，实现了 0.001 误差范围内的准确度。”

I hadn’t “sold” anyone yet, but I had accomplished something crucial: All the analysts relaxed in their chairs. Some had been holding up pens, ready to raise their arms a little higher, to interrupt with challenging questions. They put down those pens. They no longer needed to ask disruptive questions because my prewritten Status Tip-off and Flash Roll had caused them to feel reassured, thinking to themselves, “This is awesome—for once, I’m in the hands of a professional presenter who knows what he’s doing. I’m just going to sit back, listen, and enjoy the presentation.”

我还没“说服”任何人，但我完成了一件至关重要的事：所有分析师都放松地靠在椅子上。有些人之前举着笔，准备举起手臂，打断我，提出一些尖锐的问题。现在他们放下了笔。他们不再需要问那些会打断我的问题了，因为我事先准备好的状态提示和快速讲解让他们感到安心，心里想着：“太棒了——我终于遇到了一位专业的演讲者，他知道自己在做什么。我只需要放松地坐着，听着，好好享受这场演讲。”

Next, I would give them the three pieces of pre-wired information their brains were craving.

接下来，我会给他们提供他们大脑渴望的三条预先设定好的信息。

THE 3 W’S

三个W

During a presentation, you need to convey not one but three ideas to the buyer; these are the 3 W’s: Why should I care? What’s in it for me? Why you? And if you don’t want them to tune you out, you need to demand as little mental energy from them as possible while you explain these things. I’ve developed three Pre-Wired Ideas to do just that. These ideas activate the risk, reward, and fairness receptors in your listener’s brain to deliver the key pieces of information a buyer needs with a sudden whoosh of insight.

在演示过程中，你需要向买家传达三个核心信息，而不是一个。这三个信息就是三个“W”：我为什么要关心？这对我有什么好处？为什么是你？如果你不想让他们走神，就需要在解释这些内容时尽可能减少他们的注意力消耗。为此，我开发了三个“预设理念”。这些理念能够激活听众大脑中的风险、回报和公平感受器，让他们瞬间领悟到买家需要的关键信息。

Why Should I Care?

我为什么要关心？

It’s a simple matter to answer the first question using a Pre-Wired Idea called Winter Is Coming. This targets the threat receptor. An example of how this works is clearly seen in the story of Microsoft’s big comeback. When Satya Nadella took over as Microsoft’s CEO in 2014, the company relied almost exclusively on its Windows operating system for revenue. The stock price had been pretty much flat for the previous ten years and the company was seen as a has-been giant of the tech world, on its way out.

第一个问题的答案很简单，只需使用一个名为“凛冬将至”的预设理念即可。这个理念直击威胁目标。微软的强势回归就是一个很好的例子。2014年，萨蒂亚·纳德拉接任微软CEO时，公司几乎完全依赖Windows操作系统来盈利。此前十年，微软股价几乎停滞不前，被视为科技界的过气巨头，即将退出历史舞台。

Nadella had to tell the company, all 131,000 employees, that things were about to get a whole lot worse for them if they weren’t able to make the jump to cloud-based computing, and fast. He made it clear the Windows operating system was not going to keep paying the bills forever.

纳德拉不得不告诉公司全体13.1万名员工，如果他们不能尽快转型云计算，情况将会变得更加糟糕。他明确表示，Windows操作系统不可能永远支撑公司运营下去。

Winter.

冬天。

Is.

是。

Coming.

未来。

For tech giants in today’s economy, things have changed. Operating systems don’t make money. Instead, Search makes money. PCs are dead and everything is mobile. People don’t buy software; they rent it. Everything is on the cloud. If you cannot shift to cloud computing, you freeze and die. That’s what “winter” looks like for a tech giant.

在当今经济环境下，科技巨头面临的形势已经发生了变化。操作系统不再盈利，取而代之的是搜索。个人电脑已死，一切都向移动端发展。人们不再购买软件，而是选择租赁。所有数据都存储在云端。如果无法转型云计算，就会被淘汰。这就是科技巨头所面临的“寒冬”。

Nadella took major, dramatic steps to avoid Microsoft’s fast-approaching winter. First, he took an $8 billion loss to completely shut down their smartphone division. He moved Word and Excel programs to the cloud, risking another $8 billion. He spent huge portions of cash reserves building cloud computing data centers all over the world. By 2018 Microsoft’s stock price had nearly tripled, and by November of that year, the company that had recently been written off as a has-been made international front-page news by passing Apple as the most valuable company on the planet.

为了避免微软迅速逼近的寒冬，纳德拉采取了一系列重大而果断的措施。首先，他承担了80亿美元的损失，彻底关闭了智能手机部门。他将Word和Excel程序迁移到云端，又冒着损失80亿美元的风险。他还动用了大量现金储备，在全球各地建设云计算数据中心。到2018年，微软的股价几乎翻了三倍，同年11月，这家此前被认为已经过气的公司超越苹果，成为全球市值最高的公司，并登上了国际新闻头条。

What had motivated Microsoft and its 131,000 employees to pull this off? The tangible fear of being caught in a slow-moving but completely nuclear winter; the risk of total annihilation from the marketplace and the primal fear of being thrown into history’s trash bin of has-been technology. This is the Pre-Wired Idea: Those who do not perceive, prepare, adjust, and adapt to changes in the environment are wiped out. Microsoft was able to adapt and prepare for its winter, yet this company is one of the few that did. When major changes approach slowly, most businesses are caught off-guard.

是什么驱使微软及其13.1万名员工完成这项壮举？是对陷入缓慢却彻底的“核冬天”的切身恐惧；是对被市场彻底淘汰的风险，以及对被历史遗忘、沦为过时技术的原始恐惧。这就是“预先连接”理念：那些无法感知、准备、调整和适应环境变化的人将被淘汰。微软成功地适应并为“核冬天”做好了准备，但它只是少数几家做到这一点的公司之一。当重大变革缓慢到来时，大多数企业都会措手不及。

This is the Winter Is Coming narrative, and you can tell this story in any industry on any day of the week. Tell your listeners that everything they rely on to thrive today will soon be gone—and it’s happening faster than they think. Provide thoughtful examples showing why the way they run their business won’t work much longer. There’s an early winter coming, and if they don’t do anything to prepare, adjust, and adapt, they’ll get wiped out. In the meantime, others who do prepare will outlive them, step into their turf, and thrive—by taking their place in the industry, as well as their customers.

这就是“凛冬将至”的叙事，你可以在任何行业、任何一天讲述这个故事。告诉你的听众，他们赖以生存的一切很快就会消失——而且速度比他们想象的还要快。提供一些发人深省的例子，说明为什么他们目前的经营方式行不通。寒冬已至，如果他们不做好准备、调整和适应，就会被淘汰。与此同时，那些做好准备的人将会生存下来，取而代之，并在行业和客户群体中占据一席之地，蓬勃发展。

How to say this, exactly? For one, I would never tell a prospect that I have a better product than the one they are currently using, or that I have a new and better idea. I would just say that the industry as we know it is transforming, and I know how to operate successfully in this new playing field. As I like to say, the person who knows what will happen next and in the future is always the most important person in the room.

确切地说，该怎么表达呢？首先，我绝不会告诉潜在客户我的产品比他们目前使用的产品更好，或者我有一个更新更好的想法。我只会说，我们所处的行业正在经历变革，而我知道如何在这个新的竞争环境中取得成功。正如我常说的，能够预知未来趋势的人，永远是房间里最重要的人。

What’s in It for Me?

我能从中得到什么好处？

The easiest way to fully activate the “what’s in it for me” reward receptor is to use the idea of 2X: that something important to them will double in size, productivity, efficiency, competitiveness, output, or just plain happiness and satisfaction. Here’s how it works.

激活“我能从中得到什么好处”这种奖励机制的最简单方法是运用“2倍”的概念：对他们来说重要的东西将会翻倍，无论是规模、生产力、效率、竞争力、产出，还是单纯的幸福感和满足感。具体来说，就是如此。

First, take a moment to consider how often you buy a new cell phone. Manufacturers release new models at least once a year. But do you personally upgrade your phone that often? Most don’t, because the models that just came out are only slightly better than the one you bought last year. The average consumer waits a few years to buy a new cell phone, until they feel switching to a newer model will truly be a substantial upgrade. The same is true with cars. You don’t trade in your car as soon as the new model comes out every year, because the new model is at best 10 percent better than last year’s model. It’s an improvement but just isn’t worth the high cost of switching.

首先，不妨想想你多久买一次新手机。厂商每年至少会推出一款新机型。但你个人真的会这么频繁地升级手机吗？大多数人不会，因为新出的机型往往只比去年买的略好一些。普通消费者通常会等上几年才会换新手机，直到他们觉得换成新款真的能带来显著的提升。汽车也是如此。你不会每年新款上市就立刻换车，因为新款最多也就比去年的车型提升10%。虽然有所改进，但并不值得为此付出高昂的代价。

So how much of an improvement does a new product have to be over the old one for most buyers to feel like it’s worth the cost of switching? My team and I have observed that people don’t feel ready for a new car until the latest model is at least twice as good as the one they’re currently driving, miles and all. If you’re into smartphone cameras, you’ll probably wait until the new phones hold a lot more photos and deliver something like twice the photo quality in terms of resolution, sharpness, and accuracy.

那么，新产品需要比旧产品改进多少，才能让大多数买家觉得值得花钱更换呢？我和我的团队观察到，人们只有在最新款车型比他们目前驾驶的车辆（包括行驶里程）至少好两倍时，才会觉得准备好换新车。如果你喜欢智能手机拍照，你可能也会等到新款手机的拍照容量更大，并且在分辨率、清晰度和准确度方面提供两倍左右的照片质量时才会考虑购买。

Double is a magic number. Anything less than that is often not enough to activate and immediately satisfy your buyer’s reward receptor. You need to make it obvious your buyer is going to get at least twice the results in some area with your idea, product, or service—hence 2X. Conversely, show them that they will reach the same result but cut their expenses in half.

双倍是一个神奇的数字。低于这个数字往往不足以激活并立即满足买家的奖励机制。你需要让买家清楚地意识到，你的想法、产品或服务至少能在某些方面带来两倍的成果——这就是“2倍”的含义。反之，也要让他们看到，他们能以一半的成本获得同样的成果。

We’ve found that even an 80 percent improvement, while impressive, doesn’t really push people to snap to attention, hold still, and listen carefully. People are so busy with their lives that switching over to your new great way of doing things is a bit of a hassle. So you need to find some areas where your deal is providing double the value, twice the speed, or half the cost of what they have now. It can even be something subjective, such as “You’ll feel twice as good about yourself” or “Cut the hassle in half,” as long as it follows the 2X formula.

我们发现，即使提升80%，虽然令人印象深刻，但并不能真正促使人们集中注意力、保持安静并认真聆听。人们的生活节奏太快，要切换到你提出的新方法多少有些麻烦。因此，你需要找到一些方面，让你的方案能够提供双倍的价值、两倍的速度或一半的成本。这甚至可以是一些主观感受，例如“你会感觉好两倍”或“麻烦减半”，只要符合“2倍”的公式即可。

Why You?

为什么是你？

The last thing the buyer needs to know about your deal is “why you?” How can they be sure you are still going to be around after the deal is closed to handle any problems? In my world, investors want to know that the CEO of the company is not going to give up and quit when things get hard—and things always get hard. It’s not enough to say you are credible, hardworking, and trustworthy. What proof do you offer of this? What evidence can you provide to show that you’ll stick with it, work hard, stay focused, and follow through?

买家最不需要了解的就是“为什么是你？”。他们如何才能确定交易完成后你仍然会留在公司处理任何问题？在我看来，投资者想知道的是，公司CEO在遇到困难时不会放弃——而困难总是难免的。仅仅说你可靠、勤奋、值得信赖是不够的。你能提供什么证据来证明这一点？你能提供什么证据来证明你会坚持到底、努力工作、保持专注并最终达成目标？

Evolutionary psychologists Leda Cosmides and John Tooby have confirmed in their research that we all come pre-wired with a fairness receptor—in essence, a cheater-detection system. We are constantly on the lookout for cheaters and we love to see them punished—we literally crave the experience of fairness. But how do you show someone that you won’t try to game the system or quit the job early?

进化心理学家莱达·科斯米德斯和约翰·图比的研究证实，我们每个人天生都拥有公平感受器——本质上，它是一种作弊检测系统。我们时刻警惕着作弊者，并且乐于见到他们受到惩罚——我们渴望体验公平。但是，如何向别人表明你不会试图钻空子或提前离职呢？

Here’s the answer we discovered: The only way for a buyer to be 100 percent sure that you aren’t a lightweight who is going to bail when things start to get difficult is to see how heavily you are invested in a successful outcome. Your personal investment proves you have as much to lose as they do. It shows this is not a one-sided deal, and you both have skin in the game. The easiest way to help your audience to arrive at this feeling is to activate the fairness receptor using a Skin in the Game script, a quick story that shows how you are financially, physically, or contractually committed to this opportunity—maybe even more than you are asking them to be. In other words, you’ve made real sacrifices and paid the price to be here. It’s not simply that you’ll feel bad or look bad if something goes wrong; it’s that you’ll lose something important to you. The deal you’re presenting is set up so that your money, time, and professional reputation are all on the line. There has to be some evidence that you won’t stop when you’re tired—you stop when you’re done.

我们发现的答案是：买家要想百分百确定你不是那种遇到困难就临阵脱逃的软柿子，唯一的办法就是看看你对成功投入了多少。你的个人投入证明你和他们一样有风险。这表明这不是一笔单方面的交易，你们双方都承担了风险。帮助受众产生这种感觉的最简单方法是，用“风险共担”的脚本来激活他们的公平感受。这个脚本可以讲述一个简短的故事，展现你如何在经济、人力或合同层面投入到这个机会中——甚至可能比你要求他们投入的更多。换句话说，你为此做出了真正的牺牲，付出了代价。如果出了问题，你不仅仅是会感到难过或丢脸；而是你会失去一些对你来说非常重要的东西。你提出的这笔交易，意味着你的金钱、时间和职业声誉都面临风险。必须要有一些证据表明，你不会因为疲倦而停止——你会在完成任务后才停止。

When you get good at providing this Pre-Wired Idea of Skin in the Game, it’s an easy matter to combine these three concepts into a single script that includes all the elements necessary to activate the threat, reward, and fairness receptors in your buyer’s mind. In summary:

当你能够熟练地营造这种“利益攸关”的预设氛围时，就可以轻松地将这三个概念融合到一个脚本中，该脚本包含激活买家心中威胁、奖励和公平感受器所需的所有要素。总而言之：

  • The Winter Is Coming script answers “Why should I care?”
    《凛冬将至》剧本回答了“我为什么要关心？”这个问题。
  • The 2X script answers “What’s in it for me?”
    2X 脚本回答了“我能从中得到什么好处？”
  • The Skin in the Game script answers “Why you?”
    《切身利益》剧本回答了“为什么是你？”这个问题。

When I found myself standing in front of Grant Goodman and his team, I was ready to tell the story of Gennacode. This meant I was ready to activate the risk, reward, and fairness receptors in a room full of ice-cold financial analysts. But I knew before I had even started that by the time I was finished, these skeptics would have everything they needed in order to say yes to our deal.

当我站在格兰特·古德曼和他的团队面前时，我已经准备好讲述Gennacode的故事。这意味着我准备唤醒这群冷漠的金融分析师们对风险、回报和公平的感知。但我甚至在开始讲述之前就知道，等我讲完之后，这些怀疑论者将会获得所有必要的证据，从而同意我们的交易。

It was time to unleash the Pre-Wired Ideas.

是时候释放预先构思好的创意了。

THE FEELING OF “I GET IT”

“我明白了”的感觉

Jenny is the captain of her high school cheerleading squad. She’s tall, blond, energetic, and athletic. Always the center of attention, she is dating the star of the football team. She’s often cruel to other girls, especially ones who aren’t into sports, like the girls involved with band, drama, or computers.

珍妮是她所在高中啦啦队的队长。她身材高挑，金发碧眼，精力充沛，运动细胞发达。她总是人群中的焦点，而且正在和橄榄球队的明星球员交往。她经常对其他女生很刻薄，尤其是那些不热衷运动的女生，比如参加乐队、戏剧社或电脑社的女生。

Even though I gave you only around fifty words of description about Jenny, you might feel like you have a decent understanding of her character and how she would act in various situations. This isn’t an accident. It’s because Jenny fits a stock character archetype you’ve encountered over and over again in movies, books, and television shows.

虽然我只用了大约五十个字来描述珍妮，但你可能感觉对她的性格以及她在各种情况下会如何行事已经有了相当不错的了解。这并非偶然。这是因为珍妮符合你在电影、书籍和电视节目中反复遇到的一个典型人物形象。

You’ve been exposed to this “cheerleader” stereotype so many times that your brain has actually built an idea receptor for it. When you started reading the description of Jenny, your brain activated this receptor, leading to an immediate feeling of “I get it.” This is what happens when your receptors are hit, leading to a pre-wired conclusion, which is exactly what I needed to do now so that Grant Goodman and his tribe would “get it.”

你已经无数次接触过“啦啦队长”这种刻板印象，以至于你的大脑已经为此建立了一个认知受体。当你开始阅读对珍妮的描述时，你的大脑激活了这个受体，让你立刻产生“我明白了”的感觉。这就是受体被激活后发生的情况，它会引导你得出预先设定的结论，而这正是我现在需要做的，这样格兰特·古德曼和他的同伙才能“明白”。

PITCHING GENNACODE

推导 Gennacode

“Chronic diseases are the leading cause of death and disability in the United States,” I said evenly, leading with the facts. “A hundred and thirty-three million Americans have at least one chronic disease. In recent years, these numbers are going up, not down. Diabetes, Alzheimer’s, cancer, arthritis, asthma, stroke . . .”

“慢性病是美国导致死亡和残疾的主要原因，”我语气平和地说，首先列举事实。“一亿三千三百万美国人至少患有一种慢性病。近年来，这些数字不降反升。糖尿病、阿尔茨海默病、癌症、关节炎、哮喘、中风……”

Everybody looked around the room, uncomfortable with my formidable list of medical deplorables. I had them where I wanted them. Winter was coming, and they felt the stiff breeze on the back of their necks.

房间里所有人都环顾四周，面对我那份令人望而生畏的病患名单，他们感到很不自在。我完全掌控了局面。冬天即将来临，他们感到凛冽的寒风拂过后颈。

“Which one is coming for you?” I asked, looking around the room like the grim reaper seeking out his next soul. “You there.” I pointed at a quiet-looking analyst in the middle of the pack. “I bet you have the best insurance possible, right? Your insurance will cover any treatment money can buy, right?” The nervous analyst nodded. “So, what treatment will you buy if Alzheimer’s sets in? Because today, there isn’t one.” The room remained silent; everybody looked at the analyst with the potential incurable disease and looked away, as if they would turn to salt if they kept staring.

“谁要来找你？”我问道，环顾四周，如同死神寻找下一个亡魂。“就是你。”我指着人群中间一位看起来很安静的分析师。“我猜你肯定买了最好的保险，对吧？你的保险能报销任何你能花钱买到的治疗，对吧？”那位紧张的分析师点了点头。“那么，如果患上阿尔茨海默病，你会买什么治疗方案？因为目前还没有有效的治疗方法。”房间里一片寂静；每个人都看着这位可能患有不治之症的分析师，然后又赶紧移开视线，仿佛如果继续盯着他看，自己就会变成盐。

“We’re living longer than evolution designed us to,” I continued. “If you’re in this room, mathematically, your life expectancy is eighty-four. But think about it this way: Nature had already planned to wipe you out around age thirty-two. Those extra fifty-two years are an open invitation to chronic disease. And you’re not the only one who is outliving your own health limitations. There are 350 million other Americans in exactly the same boat—living too long. Unless we get advanced prediction technologies in place—and start preventing conditions before they occur—the cost of treating these diseases is going to be a third of the entire economy. That’s way too much.”

“我们的寿命比进化预期的要长，”我继续说道。“如果你在这个房间里，从数学角度来看，你的预期寿命是84岁。但换个角度想：大自然原本计划让你在32岁左右结束生命。这多出来的52年，无疑是在邀请你患上慢性疾病。而且，你并不是唯一一个超越自身健康极限的人。还有3.5亿美国人也面临着同样的问题——寿命过长。除非我们拥有先进的预测技术，并在疾病发生之前就开始预防，否则治疗这些疾病的成本将占到整个经济的三分之一。这实在是太高了。”

I was painting a bleak picture of how bad the future might be if we failed to take action now. That was my Winter Is Coming script. Next, without missing a beat, I jumped right into my 2X script. I looked over at my partner, Professor Rosenberg, and pointed to him, wagging my finger like the grim reaper.

我描绘了一幅凄凉的景象：如果我们现在不采取行动，未来将会多么糟糕。这就是我的“凛冬将至”剧本。接下来，我毫不犹豫地立刻切换到我的“2X”剧本。我看向我的搭档罗森伯格教授，指着他，像死神一样摇着手指。

“Professor Rosenberg, how old are you?”

“罗森伯格教授，您多大了？”

“Seventy-one,” he replied.

“七十一，”他回答道。

“And how are you feeling?” I asked him. “I mean, apart from some aches and pains, degrading eyesight, weight gain, hearing problems, heart palpitations, and minor memory loss. . . . Any major diseases creeping up?”

“你感觉怎么样？”我问他。“我是说，除了有些疼痛、视力下降、体重增加、听力问题、心悸和轻微的记忆力减退……还有什么大病正在慢慢发作吗？”

“No, nothing serious,” he replied, smiling, but a little hesitantly.

“不，没什么大碍，”他笑着回答，但语气中带着一丝犹豫。

“But how can you know ? I mean, these good people sitting here,” I said, pointing to the analysts, “they are seriously thinking about giving you twenty-two million dollars and then, well, maybe after they do you have a stroke the next day, or you’re diagnosed with type two diabetes, or . . . something, anything on that list of fast killer diseases. How can we assure them you’ve got another five or ten years of good energy and mental health?”

“但你怎么能知道呢？我的意思是，坐在这里的这些好心人，”我指着分析师们说，“他们认真考虑给你两千二百万美元，然后，嗯，也许他们给你钱之后，你第二天就中风了，或者被诊断出患有二型糖尿病，或者……其他什么，任何一种快速致命的疾病。我们怎么能向他们保证你还能再拥有五到十年的充沛精力和良好的精神状态呢？”

Rosenberg didn’t blink; he just stared at the audience, looking as ancient as he seemed to these whiz kids.

罗森伯格一眨不眨眼，只是盯着观众，看起来就像这些天才少年眼中的他一样古老。

“I’ve got twice the chance of living to ninety as anyone in this room,” he said.

“我活到九十岁的几率是这里任何人的两倍，”他说。

“No offense, professor,” I said skeptically, “but how are we supposed to believe that? Are you a magician? Do you have a crystal ball? Did you make a deal with the devil?” Next, I pointed at the healthiest-looking kid in the room. About thirty-two years old, muscular, he looked like a real athlete. “What about Chang?” I asked. “Look at him, and now look at yourself. Be realistic , professor—how are you going to outlast a guy who takes care of himself like that ?”

“教授，我无意冒犯，”我半信半疑地说，“但我们怎么能相信呢？您是魔术师吗？您有水晶球吗？您跟魔鬼做了交易吗？”接着，我指着房间里看起来最健康的那个孩子。他大约三十二岁，肌肉发达，看起来像个真正的运动员。“那张呢？”我问。“看看他，再看看你自己。教授，现实点吧——你怎么可能比得上一个像他那样注重保养的人呢？”

The professor smiled. This was his cue. “Until I see his genetic test, I wouldn’t bet on him. The numbers say he could be wiped off the map by the time he’s fifty. Since we’re talking numbers, I’m already twenty-one years past fifty. Nature programmed me to die long ago, but I’m still here. I’ve got a PhD in molecular biology and all the equipment a biologist could ever want at my university lab, so I’ve been treating my genetic weaknesses for decades. And now I’ve got this , which is going to let me easily hit the age of a hundred and twenty.” He held up a Gennacode test.

教授笑了。这是他该开口的信号。“在看到他的基因检测结果之前，我不会对他抱太大希望。根据统计数据，他可能活不到五十岁。说到数字，我已经过了五十岁二十一年了。大自然早就设定了我的死亡程序，但我仍然活着。我拥有分子生物学博士学位，我的大学实验室里配备了生物学家梦寐以求的所有设备，所以几十年来我一直在治疗我的基因缺陷。现在我又有了这个，它能让我轻松活到一百二十岁。”他举起一张基因检测报告。

I took the test out of the professor’s hand and showed it to the New Icon audience. “Twice as likely to live to ninety as anyone in here,” I said thoughtfully, repeating the first word for emphasis: “ Twice as likely. Thanks to a single test. Going to live to a hundred and twenty unless he gets hit by a bus.

我从教授手中接过试卷，展示给“新偶像”的听众。“活到九十岁的概率是这里任何人的两倍，”我若有所思地说，为了强调，我重复了一遍第一个字：“两倍的概率。这都多亏了一次测试。除非被车撞了，否则他肯定能活到一百二十岁。”

“And since we are contemplating Professor Rosenberg’s mortality at present, and now that we are all reassured that he’s going to be around for another couple years, let’s get to know him a little bit better,” I continued. “You can see the highlights of his professional career on his biography, but that doesn’t tell the whole story. Sure, it lists all of the academic honors and high-profile speeches he’s delivered. But it doesn’t show his 1-bedroom apartment in Mid-Wilshire, the 1994 Toyota Corolla he drives, a closet full of cheap black suits from Eastern Europe like this one he’s wearing now, and this—I pointed to his wrist—an eighteen-dollar Casio calculator watch. And he flew here on Southwest Airlines in Group C. I know, I sat next to him in seat 37B, worst seat ever—it’s by the toilet. Is the professor just cheap? Where’s all the money he’s earned from all those prestigious awards? There should be at least $5 million somewhere. What happened to it all?

“既然我们现在正在思考罗森伯格教授的生命，而且现在我们都确信他还能再活几年，那么让我们更好地了解他一些吧，”我继续说道。 “他的传记里列举了他职业生涯的亮点，但这并不能说明全部。没错，传记里列出了他获得的所有学术荣誉和发表过的重要演讲。但传记里没写他在中威尔希尔区那套一居室公寓里的生活，他开的那辆1994年的丰田卡罗拉，他衣橱里塞满了从东欧买来的廉价黑色西装（就像他现在穿的这套），还有——我指着他的手腕——一块价值18美元的卡西欧计算器手表。而且他乘坐西南航空公司的C组航班来的。我知道，因为我就坐在他旁边的37B座位上，那是我坐过的最差的座位——就在厕所旁边。这位教授是不是太抠门了？他从那些声名显赫的奖项里赚的钱都到哪儿去了？至少应该有500万美元吧。这些钱都到哪儿去了？”

“Let me clear up this minor mystery,” I said to the crowd. “You’ve heard Professor Rosenberg speak here today. You’ve seen his work. You know the kind of organizations that have awarded him their highest honor. So I’m sure you already know exactly where all his money is—all five point seven million. Of course. It’s in Gennacode. So are twelve patents, fifteen years of research, assistance from twenty-five PhD colleagues around the world, and most important, the tireless dedication of this man right here.” Professor Rosenberg looked up at the people in the room, and I swear he almost blushed. Everyone was smiling at him.

“让我来解开这个小小的谜团，”我对在场的众人说道，“你们今天都听过罗森伯格教授的演讲，看过他的研究成果，也知道是哪些机构授予了他最高荣誉。所以我相信你们肯定都清楚他那五十七万美元的去向。当然，它们都在Gennacode里。还有他的十二项专利、十五年的研究成果、来自世界各地二十五位博士同事的协助，以及最重要的——他本人孜孜不倦的奉献精神。”罗森伯格教授抬头看了看房间里的人，我发誓他差点脸红了。每个人都面带微笑地看着他。

“It’s interesting how just a few years ago you would have looked at Gennacode and said, ‘Hmm, this is nothing but a ragtag group of international PhD misfits, really just a bunch of “teaching” professors who are playing with genetics, trying to crack the uncrackable code of chronic disease, pretty much the same old story that never goes anywhere,’” I said, rounding third toward home base. “Because when it was just research and math and late-night conference calls about the BRCA genes or the BioSequence technology, we didn’t call you for money. The professor wrote those checks month after month, year after year. Real money. Five million bucks. But today, you’re looking at the number one company in the field employing high-density microarrays, nanorobotics, and pooled-library multiplexing . . . and a patented enzyme-catalyzed DNA polymerization process.

“很有意思的是，就在几年前，你看到Gennacode公司时可能会说，‘嗯，这不过是一群乌合之众般的国际博士，一群只会‘教书’的教授，玩弄遗传学，试图破解慢性病的神秘密码，老一套，永远也成不了什么名堂，’”我一边说着，一边绕过三垒向终点线走去。“因为以前，我们只是做研究、搞数学、开深夜电话会议讨论BRCA基因或BioSequence技术，那时候我们可没找你要钱。教授们年复一年、月复一月地给我们开支票。真金白银。五百万美元。但如今，你看到的却是该领域首屈一指的公司，它运用了高密度微阵列、纳米机器人和混合文库多重测序技术……以及一项获得专利的酶催化DNA聚合工艺。”

“And we did all that without you. . . . Now let’s cover what we can do with you.”

“我们之前做这一切都是没有你的……现在让我们来看看有了你我们能做些什么。”

I looked around the room and I could tell I’d been successful. The geniuses were silent. Instead of suspicion, they were looking at Rosenberg and me with understanding and appreciation, but we weren’t assured of a $20 million check yet. I had to show them what was in it for them. I flipped the projector to show a graph of the company’s finances. Thanks to careful spending and a couple of important partnerships with established hospitals, Gennacode already had more than $5 million in revenue.

我环顾四周，知道自己成功了。那些天才们沉默不语。他们没有怀疑，而是带着理解和赞赏的目光看着我和罗森伯格，但我们还没能确定能拿到那张两千万美元的支票。我必须让他们明白他们能从中获得什么好处。我切换投影仪，展示了公司的财务图表。得益于谨慎的支出以及与几家知名医院建立的重要合作关系，Gennacode 的收入已经超过了五百万美元。

“Of course,” I said, pulling a large stack of folded pages out of my bag, “at the end of the day, what you folks want to know most about is this.” I shook the pages in my hand for emphasis, increasing our perceived value and adding to the intrigue with this one gesture alone. “How big can this company get, and how fast? What does the revenue pipeline look like? Will sales be ten million? A hundred million? A billion? I’ll tell you right now, it looks pretty darn good. So let’s put some hard numbers around this opportunity.”

“当然，”我一边说着，一边从包里掏出一大叠折叠好的文件，“说到底，你们最想知道的还是这个。”我晃了晃手中的文件以示强调，这一个动作就提升了我们公司的价值，也增添了不少神秘感。“这家公司能发展到多大规模？发展速度有多快？营收前景如何？销售额能达到一千万？一亿？十亿？我可以坦白地告诉你们，前景相当不错。所以，让我们用具体的数字来分析一下这个机会吧。”

The thick stack of papers quickly made its way around the table. There was a folder for everyone in the room—fifteen dossiers in all. But what nobody realized was that I had a small psychological tactic up my sleeve. These weren’t typical hard-to-understand financial projections and reports. Instead, I had printed all the company’s contracts, deals, and prospective customer agreements, organized in perfect, tidy rows, exactly as analysts would do for themselves. The revenue projections were atmospherically large, and in each folder I had taped together sixteen sheets of paper like a giant origami map to the secrets of the universe. I had provided information in a way that the analysts were pre-wired to receive it.

厚厚一叠文件迅速在桌上传来传去。房间里的每个人都有一份文件夹——总共十五份文件。但没人意识到，我其实留了一手心理战术。这些并非晦涩难懂的财务预测和报告。相反，我把公司所有的合同、交易和潜在客户协议都打印出来，整齐有序地排列成行，就像分析师自己整理文件一样。收入预测的数字高得惊人，每个文件夹里我都用胶带把十六张纸粘在一起，就像一张巨大的折纸地图，揭示着宇宙的奥秘。我以一种分析师们早已习惯的方式呈现信息。

As these impossibly large folded printouts were passed around the table and everyone grabbed a copy, the room started to fill with the rustle of paper. And then when the volumes of revenue projections—with supporting evidence of contracts and customer invoices—were unfolded simultaneously, the room got louder, and you could feel the positive energy crackle. I could hear analysts exclaiming, “Oh, this is so cool, check this out!” Paper was flying everywhere as they tried to fully comprehend the extraordinary revenue projections. Everyone loved it—except Grant Goodman, who rose from his chair, glaring at his happy geniuses with dismay.

当这些折叠得惊人的打印件在桌上传递，每个人都拿了一份时，房间里开始充斥着纸张的沙沙声。然后，当这些厚厚的收入预测——连同合同和客户发票等佐证材料——同时展开时，房间里的喧闹声更大了，你能感觉到一股积极的能量在涌动。我听到分析师们惊呼：“哇，这太棒了，快看！”他们试图完全理解这些惊人的收入预测，纸张四处飞舞。每个人都很兴奋——除了格兰特·古德曼，他从椅子上站起来，沮丧地瞪着他那些得意洋洋的天才们。

“Isn’t anybody going to ask this guy some due diligence questions?” he asked, pointing menacingly around the room. No one said a word. The analysts just shrugged. They had nothing. I had checked every single box. I looked up and saw Grant Goodman staring at me with the most peculiar look on his face. It was like I was David Copperfield and had just made the Statue of Liberty disappear.

“难道没人打算问问这家伙一些尽职调查的问题吗？”他问道，同时恶狠狠地环顾四周。没人说话。分析师们只是耸了耸肩。他们一无所知。我已经把所有条件都满足了。我抬起头，看到格兰特·古德曼正用一种极其怪异的表情盯着我。感觉就像我成了大卫·科波菲尔，刚刚让自由女神像消失了一样。

An hour later the CFO came out of the deliberation room and looked at the professor and me, stunned. “I don’t know what happened in there,” he said. “But we just voted to invest in Gennacode, and it’s going to be the largest single investment our firm has ever made.”

一小时后，首席财务官从会议室出来，震惊地看着我和教授。“我不知道里面发生了什么，”他说，“但我们刚刚投票决定投资Gennacode，这将是我们公司有史以来最大的一笔单笔投资。”

At no point did I tell these investors what to think, or even ask them directly to invest. They decided to do so on their own. This is the awesome power of Pre-Wired Ideas. Even at a circus of money like New Icon, in front of this group of global sophisticates, you can count on everyone—no matter who they are, what their training is, or where they went to school—to respond in exactly the same way when you activate their Pre-Wired Idea receptors.

我从未告诉这些投资者该怎么想，甚至从未直接要求他们投资。他们完全是自愿做出决定的。这就是预设观念的强大力量。即使在像New Icon这样资金密集的场合，面对着这群来自世界各地的精英，你也能确信，无论他们是谁，接受过什么培训，毕业于哪所学校，只要你激活了他们预设观念的接收器，他们都会做出完全相同的反应。

#↩

CHAPTER 5

第五章

The Power of Plain Vanilla

纯香草的力量

M y partner Logan is a deal junkie. What kind of deals?

我的合伙人洛根是个购物狂。他喜欢买什么样的折扣呢？

Here’s a good example. A few years ago we tried to buy the Chicago White Sox because Logan thought we could get the team for a steal. After that, we did buy a $40 million airplane for $30 million, which was a great deal . . . until I realized a single tank of gas was costing me $90,000 and it burned 380 gallons an hour. We bought seventeen hotels, such as the Doubletree in downtown Houston. And then we tried to buy the Tower Records building right out from under the company. Their CEO had a few things to say about that. One of them was no.

举个例子。几年前，我们想收购芝加哥白袜队，因为洛根觉得我们可以捡个大便宜。之后，我们确实以3000万美元的价格买了一架价值4000万美元的飞机，这笔交易当时看起来非常划算……直到我发现加满一箱油就要花掉我9万美元，而且每小时要烧掉380加仑。我们收购了17家酒店，比如休斯顿市中心的希尔顿逸林酒店。然后，我们又想从Tower Records公司手里直接买下他们的大楼。他们的CEO对此有很多话要说，其中一句就是“不”。

Logan goes further, pushes harder, and looks for more creative ways to get a deal done than anyone else out there. We have done twenty-seven deals together, and I always tell him, “This is the last one,” but it never is.

洛根比任何人都更积极主动、更努力，也更善于寻找创造性的方法来促成交易。我们合作完成了二十七笔交易，我总是跟他说“这是最后一笔了”，但结果总是并非如此。

The Logan Deals, as I’ve started calling them, are like action movies starring a venture capitalist with a go-anywhere jet and a no-holds-barred approach to life. He is always looking for his next big win, no matter where it takes him or what the risks. And in a crucial respect, Logan has turned the deal game on its head, because he isn’t looking for less risk, he’s looking for more—for him, the more millions on the line, the better. If $1 million is good, $10 million is great, and $100 million is golden. He’s a man with only two settings: fast and asleep. From the outside, Logan is a well-composed model of success and makes it look easy to be a mogul—but I know firsthand, being a kingpin is far from easy, and closing a deal with him is a bumpy and dangerous ride. What follows is the story of one of the wildest rides I’ve ever taken.

我开始把这些交易称为“洛根交易”，它们就像动作片，主角是一位拥有私人飞机、行事作风无所畏惧的风险投资家。他总是渴望下一个大赢家，无论风险有多大，也不管会把他带到哪里。在关键的一点上，洛根彻底颠覆了交易游戏的规则，因为他追求的不是降低风险，而是提高风险——对他来说，投资金额越大越好。如果100万美元算好，1000万美元算好，1亿美元算金子。他只有两种状态：要么全力以赴，要么昏昏欲睡。从表面上看，洛根是成功人士的典范，让人觉得当大亨轻而易举——但我亲身经历过，成为行业巨头绝非易事，与他达成交易更是一场颠簸而危险的旅程。接下来，我将讲述我经历过的最惊险刺激的一次交易。

Over the course of my dealmaking career, I’ve observed that most people proceed cautiously when chasing a huge financial reward, because they know there is a very real possibility of great loss. This is why most people will pause at critical moments to think about everything that could go wrong (especially when millions of dollars are on the line) and weigh the potential reversal of fortune before they decide whether to go forward.

在我多年的交易生涯中，我观察到大多数人在追求巨额经济回报时都会谨慎行事，因为他们深知存在遭受巨大损失的现实可能性。正因如此，大多数人在关键时刻都会停下来，仔细思考所有可能出错的情况（尤其是在涉及数百万美元的情况下），权衡利弊，然后再决定是否继续推进。

Not Logan. He immediately goes all-in pretty much without blinking. When Logan is chasing a deal, let’s just say he’s either in or he’s out; there’s no maybe.

洛根可不是这样。他几乎毫不犹豫地全力以赴。洛根一旦决定做成一笔交易，要么就全力以赴，要么就彻底放弃，没有模棱两可的余地。

And this is why I found myself with Logan one Wednesday morning walking through a Chinese marketplace in Honolulu. The Mahalo Marketplace, in the heart of historic downtown Honolulu, is a busy plaza, boasting a colorful combination of shops, an indoor market, and the gastronomic smells of an ethnic food court. We’d arrived on Logan’s jet an hour before and he still hadn’t told me what the deal was, but I was bracing myself for the moment he revealed his grand plan.

这就是为什么某个星期三的早晨，我发现自己和洛根一起漫步在檀香山的一个中国市场里。马哈洛市场位于檀香山历史悠久的市中心，是一个熙熙攘攘的广场，汇集了各式各样的商店、一个室内市场，以及弥漫着各国美食香气的美食广场。我们一个小时前乘坐洛根的私人飞机抵达，他仍然没有告诉我究竟是怎么回事，但我已经做好了准备，等待他揭晓他的宏伟计划。

We pushed past a long line of aggressive shoppers at the Tea Hut, walked in front of a small stand with a great selection of exotic spices for purchase, such as “monkey gland five salt ghost pepper mix,” and stopped at the Long Life Noodles stall. Logan claimed these would enhance my mental and physical powers.

我们挤过茶馆前排着的长长的、熙熙攘攘的购物队伍，走到一个摆满了琳琅满目异域香料的小摊前，比如“猴腺五盐鬼椒粉”，然后停在了长寿面摊前。洛根声称这些面条能增强我的身心力量。

Was this why we had flown here from Beverly Hills?

这就是我们从比佛利山庄飞来这里的原因吗？

“Oren, this place is so timeless. . . . It’s iconic . . . legendary.” (You have to know Logan to understand all this means just one thing: profitable.) He continued, “You can find anything! Look, there—every possible kind of Chinese tea. And right there, dragon’s tooth dumplings. And there, shrimp so fresh they are still moving when they serve ’em to you.”

“奥伦，这地方真是太经典了……简直是标志性的……传奇般的存在。”（你得了解洛根才能明白，这一切只有一个含义：赚钱。）他接着说，“你什么都能找到！瞧，那儿——各种各样的中国茶。还有那儿，龙牙饺子。还有那儿，鲜虾，端上来的时候还在动。”

Logan had a point. The place was packed with customers. There were lines stretching fifteen deep and more for some of the food vendors. I tried to figure out what he had in mind. It sounded like maybe he wanted to buy a noodle company and franchise it. The two of us had started business ventures in dozens of zip codes over the years, so there was no telling what he might be thinking now. But he was mainly a real estate guy, so noodles seemed a little off base.

洛根说得有道理。店里挤满了顾客，有些小吃摊前排起了长队，足足有十五人甚至更多。我努力揣摩他的心思。听起来他好像想买一家面条公司，然后做连锁加盟。这些年来，我们俩在几十个不同的邮政编码地区都创业过，所以谁也说不准他现在在想什么。但他主要还是做房地产的，所以面条生意似乎有点不搭调。

“I’ve never seen anything like this anywhere,” Logan continued. “And wait until you see the rent roll . There’s a yearlong waiting list to get in, five percent annual rent bumps, and the owner keeps two percent of total sales.”

“我从未见过这样的情况，”洛根继续说道。“等你看到租金账单就知道了。想租到这样的房子得排队等一年，租金每年上涨5%，而且房东还要抽取总销售额的2%。”

I gave a long, low whistle, the kind finance people do when they see a good deal and impressive numbers.

我吹了一声长长的、低沉的口哨，就像金融界人士看到好交易和令人印象深刻的数字时会吹的那种口哨一样。

“So what’s the deal?” I asked, trying to sound casual as I dug into my soup. Logan’s deals always seemed to involve large sums of money and crazy timelines, so I was right to be worried about what was coming next.

“所以到底是怎么回事？”我一边大口喝着汤，一边尽量装作若无其事地问道。洛根的交易似乎总是牵涉到巨额资金和疯狂的时间安排，所以我担心接下来会发生什么，这并非没有道理。

Beaming, Logan said, “We can step in to buy the whole thing for forty-two million. We’ll acquire it into a special-purpose entity, have our guys update the financials, do a big cleanup, then you sell it for at least fifty million. What do you say, are you in or out?” I almost choked on a Long Life noodle. So that was the deal. We had done a number of these types of real estate deals together already, and making money this way was a simple formula—when it worked:

洛根笑容满面地说：“我们可以出价四千二百万美元把整个项目买下来。我们会把它收购到一个特殊目的实体里，让我们的人更新财务报表，进行一次彻底的清理，然后你至少能卖五千万美元。怎么样，你加入还是退出？”我差点被一根长寿牌方便面噎住。就这样，交易达成了。我们之前已经合作做过好几笔类似的房地产交易，这种赚钱方式的公式很简单——如果一切顺利的话：

Step 1: Buy a profitable and stable retail center.
第一步：收购一家盈利且稳定的零售中心。
Step 2: Put a few million in to fix it up.
第二步：投入几百万美元进行修缮。
Step 3: Re-calculate the financial projections.
步骤 3：重新计算财务预测。
Step 4: Sell it and keep a few million for ourselves.
第四步：卖掉它，自己留下几百万美元。

But we had never put ourselves on the hook for anywhere near $40 million. That was a ton of money. And Mahalo was anything but a slam dunk.

但我们从未让自己背负过接近4000万美元的债务。那是一笔巨款。而且，《Mahalo》这部电影也绝非十拿九稳就能成功。

“I don’t know, Logan,” I said, feeling a bit hesitant. To be blunt, I didn’t like the idea of shrimp that still moved when you were eating them, let alone stalls full of fish heads. Plus, I mean, it was Logan. Sure, he’s a total bloodhound for deals that have been overlooked by others, ones that are quirky and profitable, but you could always be sure something would come up that wasn’t expected.

“我不知道，洛根，”我有些犹豫地说。坦白讲，我不喜欢吃还在动的虾，更别提摆满鱼头的摊位了。而且，那可是洛根啊。没错，他确实能找到别人忽略的、奇特又有利可图的交易，但你总能预料到，总会有一些意想不到的事情发生。

“Forty-two million,” I reminded him, shaking my head. “That’s a lot of dough.”

“四千二百万，”我摇摇头提醒他，“那可是一大笔钱。”

There was another reason I wasn’t jumping in quite as eagerly as I might have in the past: My wife and our little boy were back in California, living in a nice but small apartment while I was having our family home built in Rancho Santa Fe. The house had started as a simple project, but with the extra two garages, hockey rink, and expanded kitchen the expenses were climbing fast. In fact, just that morning, Vince, the kitchen guy, had been trying to call me about a copper hood for the La Cornue stove he had picked out, but I didn’t answer. I knew those stoves were at least twenty-five thousand dollars and I wasn’t in the mood to hear about it.

还有另一个原因让我没有像以前那样积极参与：我的妻子和小儿子回到了加州，住在一套虽小但不错的公寓里，而我正在兰乔圣菲建造我们的家。房子最初只是个简单的项目，但随着两个额外的车库、一个冰球场和一个扩建的厨房，开销迅速攀升。事实上，就在那天早上，负责厨房装修的文斯还试图打电话给我，询问他选中的那台拉科努（La Cornue）炉灶的铜质抽油烟机，但我没接。我知道那些炉灶至少要两万五千美元，我实在没心情听人唠叨。

So Logan’s proposal was coming at a terrible time for me. I absolutely could not afford to get locked up in a huge deal right now.

所以洛根的求婚对我来说真是雪上加霜。我现在绝对负担不起任何巨额债务。

“Yeah, but I mean look at this place,” he said, waving his hands and looking me dead in the eye. “It’s got all the historic bona fides you could ask for. People are going to love it. And it’s one of a kind, Oren. The competition can’t build another one because, you know, where would you put it? They’re not making any more Hawaii.”

“是啊，不过你看这地方，”他一边挥着手，一边直视着我的眼睛说，“它拥有你所能想到的一切历史底蕴。人们一定会喜欢它的。而且它是独一无二的，奥伦。竞争对手根本建不出第二个，因为，你知道，你能把它建在哪儿呢？夏威夷可不是个例。”

He had me there. In Texas, you can just build another strip mall two hundred feet down the road from the last one. If you tried that here, you’d end up in the ocean. “All right,” I said, since I really didn’t want to throw cold water on Logan’s enthusiasm. “I’ll take a look at the numbers.”

他这话把我说服了。在德克萨斯州，你可以在离上一个商业街两百英尺远的地方再建一个。要是在这里这么做，最终只会落得个烂摊子。“好吧，”我说，因为我实在不想浇灭洛根的热情。“我会看看数据。”

Upon further analysis, this deal didn’t look bad at all. I was pretty confident I’d be able to sell the place for $50 million, leaving Logan and me to pocket a cool $6 million for the trouble.

仔细分析之后，这笔交易看起来还不错。我很有信心能以5000万美元的价格卖掉这栋房子，这样我和洛根就能净赚600万美元。

“OK,” I said after scanning the numbers. “I’m definitely in. But do not sign anything until I can run it by a few investors and make sure there’s a market for this monster, so just hold tight.” I shot Logan an “I mean it” look so intense that it stopped him mid-noodle. The idea of not moving ahead was not really in his playbook.

“好的，”我扫了一眼数字后说道，“我肯定加入。不过，在我找几个投资人讨论过，确认这个项目有市场之前，别签任何东西，所以你先等等。”我狠狠地瞪了洛根一眼，眼神坚定得让他愣住了。他可没想过要放弃。

“All right,” he said, going back to chewing again. “But hurry up; we need to make this happen fast.”

“好吧，”他一边说着，一边又开始咀嚼。“不过要快点，我们得尽快完成这件事。”

TEST-MARKETING THE MARKETPLACE

市场测试

When entrepreneurs build new apps or websites, they don’t have to do much testing before jumping in with both feet and going for it. The total risk involved in building a prototype web application is about fifty thousand dollars. In the business world, this is a small price to pay for launching a product that could be worth millions. This way of doing things is called MVP, or minimum viable product, and it works great when you’re writing software in your kitchen with your two best friends and a guy you met at Starbucks. But when you graduate to working a deal worth tens of millions of dollars, “Just do it” is not a great motto. In these situations, when the potential risk is complete financial meltdown, you need to employ a more disciplined process of due diligence.

创业者开发新应用或网站时，往往无需进行太多测试就贸然投入，全力以赴。开发一个原型网页应用的总风险大约为五万美元。在商业世界里，为了推出一款可能价值数百万美元的产品，这点成本微不足道。这种做法被称为MVP，即最小可行产品。当你和两个最好的朋友以及在星巴克认识的一个人在厨房里编写软件时，这种方法非常有效。但当你要处理价值数千万美元的交易时，“Just do it”（只管去做）就不是一句好格言了。在这种情况下，潜在风险可能是彻底的财务崩溃，你需要采用更加严谨的尽职调查流程。

The reason I was hesitant to jump in with Mahalo Marketplace is the exceptionally high risk. If I wasn’t able to find investors who wanted to buy into the deal, then Logan and I would be “locked up,” and we’d have to buy it ourselves. In other words, I would own a fifty-two-thousand-square-foot Chinese marketplace, while the bank would come take my house, my cars, and my prized 1975 Roger Staubach game-worn jersey. This was definitely something to be avoided.

我之所以迟迟不敢投资Mahalo Marketplace，是因为风险极高。如果找不到愿意投资的投资者，我和Logan就得“被套牢”，只能自己掏腰包买下它。换句话说，我将拥有一个占地52000平方英尺的中国市场，而银行则会来收回我的房子、车子，还有我珍藏的1975年罗杰·斯托巴克比赛球衣。这绝对是我要避免的。

To avoid major missteps I have developed a methodical system for conducting due diligence on my deals, as does every company that’s about to spend $40 million. Nothing fancy, it’s called a Deal Viability Test, and you don’t need an MBA or a degree in statistics to figure it out.

为了避免重大失误，我制定了一套系统化的尽职调查流程来处理我的交易，就像所有即将花费4000万美元的公司一样。这套流程并不复杂，叫做交易可行性测试，你不需要MBA学位或统计学学位就能理解它。

Step 1: Speak with about ten potential buyers.
第一步：与大约十位潜在买家交谈。
Step 2: Tell them about the best features of the product.
第二步：向他们介绍产品的最佳功能。
Step 3: Gauge interest.
步骤 3：评估兴趣。
Step 4: Decide if there’s enough demand to keep going.
第四步：决定是否有足够的需求继续下去。

That’s it. I simply pull out my phone, call up a handful of my go-to investors, tell them about whatever deal I’m currently working on, and test their interest. Typically, these are the results:

就是这样。我只需拿出手机，给几个常用的投资人打个电话，告诉他们我目前正在进行的项目，然后试探一下他们的兴趣。通常情况下，结果如下：

  • Two or three will say, Yes, I want in!
    两三个人会说：“是的，我想加入！”
  • A few others will say, It’s interesting; tell me more.
    还有一些人会说：“这很有意思，请详细说说。”
  • The rest will say, I told you last time, lose my number!
    其他人会说：“我上次就告诉过你，别再联系我了！”

If you’re lucky enough to get a few yeses on these brief calls, you can move ahead and push the deal through, confident that there is a market for whatever it is you are trying to sell. But I wasn’t at all prepared for the response I got about the Mahalo Marketplace.

如果你足够幸运，在这些简短的电话中得到几个肯定的答复，你就可以继续推进交易，并确信无论你卖什么，都有市场。但我完全没有预料到Mahalo Marketplace会得到这样的反响。

I sent out an initial query email to some investors to gauge their interest and in less than a half hour I had fifteen calls scheduled—completely unheard of in a business where it’s notoriously hard to get decision makers on the phone. And the responses I got to the email were very positive, even enthusiastic.

我给一些投资者发了一封初步的询价邮件，想了解他们的兴趣。不到半小时，我就安排了十五个电话——这在业内简直闻所未闻，因为众所周知，要联系到决策者非常困难。而且，我收到的邮件回复都非常积极，甚至可以说是热情洋溢。

“Would love to hear more about this. 3:30pm EST?”

“很想了解更多详情。美国东部时间下午3:30可以吗？”

“Very interested. Call me between 3 and 4:30.”

“非常感兴趣。请在下午3点到4点半之间给我打电话。”

“Intriguing. Talk now?”

“有意思。现在可以聊吗？”

I texted Logan, “Initial response is GREAT,” and “Will call you after I talk to everyone.” He replied with a smiley face.

我给洛根发短信说：“初步反响非常好”，“等我跟大家都谈过之后再给你打电话”。他回复了一个笑脸表情。

For the rest of the afternoon and evening I was on the phone with a total of sixteen investors, and as I made the calls one by one, an interesting pattern started to emerge. In fact, every investor I talked to said exactly the same thing: They loved it conceptually , because it was Hawaii, but in reality it had no Starbucks or Jamba Juice and it had some pretty bad Yelp reviews, so in the end: Nope, not for me. Yeah, it’s a cool project, but real quirky; sorry, not interested. It was definitely cool and exciting, and Hawaii is always worth looking at, but it seemed too much of a risk when they started to learn more about the small vendors, the lack of a Starbucks, and the open-air food stalls. When push came to shove they would rather stick with the tried-and-true.

整个下午和晚上，我都在和总共十六位投资人通电话。随着电话一个个打过来，一个有趣的模式开始显现。事实上，我跟每位投资人都说了同样的话：他们很喜欢这个项目的概念，因为它是在夏威夷，但实际上那里既没有星巴克也没有Jamba Juice，而且Yelp上的评价也很差，所以最终他们还是放弃了。没错，这的确是个很酷的项目，但太古怪了；抱歉，我没兴趣。这个项目确实很酷很刺激，夏威夷也总是值得关注的，但当他们开始了解更多关于小商贩、缺少星巴克以及露天食品摊位的信息时，他们觉得风险太大了。最终，他们还是更倾向于选择那些久经考验的可靠项目。

Whew. I let out an enormous sigh when I hung up with the final investor and crossed his name off the list. I should have been disappointed by the overall response, but in truth I was relieved. My test-marketing strategy had just saved Logan and me from making a huge mistake.

呼。挂断最后一位投资人的电话，把他的名字从名单上划掉时，我长长地舒了一口气。我本该对整体反响感到失望，但实际上我松了一口气。我的测试营销策略刚刚避免了我和洛根犯下一个巨大的错误。

“I told you, Logan, when it comes to big deals, you have to be cautious. Until I talk to actual buyers, I believe none of what I hear and half of what I see,” I said over drinks a short while later. “Turns out there’s no way we’d have been able to sell that thing for fifty million. Every investor I talked to said they wouldn’t go near it.” I pointed a finger at Logan. “This is why we do things my way: careful, scientific, and logical. Measure twice, cut once.”

“我早就跟你说过，洛根，做大生意一定要谨慎。在真正跟买家谈之前，我听到的我一个字都不信，看到的我也只能信一半。”过了一会儿，我一边喝酒一边说。“结果证明，我们根本不可能以五千万的价格卖掉那东西。我问过的每个投资人都说他们根本不会碰它。”我指着洛根说，“这就是为什么我们做事要按我的方式来：谨慎、科学、合乎逻辑。三思而后行。”

Logan listened intently. He hadn’t touched his spicy tuna roll or said a single word for about two minutes. I understood how he felt. Giving up on a deal you’ve spent time on is one of the most difficult things about this business. Logan had worked with this seller for months and this would be a tough one to get over. I knew he was feeling let down.

洛根全神贯注地听着。他一口辣金枪鱼卷都没动，也一句话没说，已经两分钟了。我理解他的感受。放弃一笔你投入了大量时间的交易，是这行里最难的事情之一。洛根和这位卖家合作了好几个月，这件事对他来说无疑是难以释怀的。我知道他很失望。

“Hey, it’s OK,” I said reassuringly. “I know you worked hard to line this up. Every deal can’t be a winner. We’ll find another one.”

“嘿，没关系，”我安慰道，“我知道你为了促成这件事付出了很多努力。不可能每笔交易都成功。我们会找到其他的。”

Logan shook his head. “You’re such a hypochondriac,” he said in an excited voice, using the term incorrectly. “I already bought the property. I signed the purchase agreement this afternoon.”

洛根摇了摇头。“你真是个疑病症患者，”他激动地说，用词不当。“我已经买下那处房产了。我今天下午签了购房协议。”

“You—you . . . What? ”

“你——你……什么？”

Logan had specifically promised not to do this, and promised it over Long Life Noodles!

洛根曾明确承诺不会这样做，而且他还用长寿面条来保证过！

“Well, that pre-research stuff is always just a formality! You always do it and it’s always a green light. Maybe you did it wrong this time.” His voice trailed off and we sat there in silence. We both knew what the research meant.

“哎，那些前期调研什么的，不过是走个形式而已！你每次都做了，而且每次都万事大吉。也许这次你做错了。”他的声音渐渐低了下去，我们沉默地坐在那里。我们俩都明白调研的意义。

We were now locked up and on the hook for exactly $42 million.

我们被关了起来，还得偿还整整 4200 万美元。

I thought about the house I was having built on the other side of the ocean in California and how it would be repossessed before the dream kitchen was even finished.

我想起了我在加州大洋彼岸建造的房子，以及在梦想中的厨房还没完工之前，它就会被收回。

SQUIRREL THEORY AND THE SCIENCE OF NOVELTY

松鼠理论与新奇科学

Have you ever watched a squirrel explore a piece of litter, a picnic basket, or something left on the ground with food in it? The squirrel has a very specific method to handle this situation. He gets about five feet away, which is as close as he can get to the thing without having a total panic attack. Then he pauses on hind legs for a moment to think and compose himself. Now back on all fours, he advances farther, ever so cautiously getting just . . . a little . . . closer, but then panic! —he scurries away to hide in a bush. After a brief time-out, he gets the nerve to approach again and gets a little closer this time until, once again— pitter patter —he scurries away. This process is repeated many times until finally the squirrel is close enough to stick his head into the bag of chips or whatever it is and discover what is inside. At last, his curiosity is satisfied.

你有没有观察过松鼠探索垃圾、野餐篮，或者地上其他装有食物的东西？松鼠处理这种情况有一套独特的方法。它会先离目标大约五英尺远，这是它能靠近的极限距离，再近就会惊慌失措。然后它会用后腿站立片刻，思考并调整心态。接着，它四肢着地，小心翼翼地继续前进，一点点靠近目标，但突然又惊慌失措——它赶紧溜进灌木丛躲藏起来。短暂的冷静之后，它鼓起勇气再次靠近，这次又靠近了一些，直到——嗖嗖嗖——它再次溜走。这个过程反复多次，直到最后，松鼠终于可以把头伸进薯片袋或其他什么东西里，看看里面有什么。最终，它的好奇心得到了满足。

This pattern of behavior is remarkably consistent, whether it’s a rabbit, rat, squirrel, or raccoon. And whenever we explore something new, it turns out, it’s the same for humans.

这种行为模式非常一致，无论是兔子、老鼠、松鼠还是浣熊。而且，每当我们探索新事物时，都会发现人类也是如此。

After receiving his PhD from Yale in 1951, a young English psychologist named Daniel Berlyne became fascinated with this type of exploratory behavior and dedicated his career to studying it. By subjecting rats to a variety of experiments, he discovered that exploratory behavior changes dramatically depending on the type of object the animal is pursuing. The more radically new and exciting something is, the more likely it is that the animal will decide to explore it . . . but only to a certain extent. Berlyne’s research shows that exploratory behavior reaches a peak at a moderate level of novelty and then starts to decline. In other words, mammal brains seem to like things that are a little new and unusual, but not so new and unusual that we don’t know how to process it.

1951年，年轻的英国心理学家丹尼尔·伯林（Daniel Berlyne）在耶鲁大学获得博士学位后，便对这种探索行为产生了浓厚的兴趣，并毕生致力于研究。他通过对老鼠进行各种实验，发现探索行为会根据动物追逐的物体类型而发生显著变化。事物越是新颖刺激，动物就越有可能去探索……但这种探索也仅限于一定程度。伯林的研究表明，探索行为在新奇程度适中时达到顶峰，然后开始下降。换句话说，哺乳动物的大脑似乎喜欢略带新奇和不寻常的事物，但又不能太新奇到让我们无法理解和处理。

Why, exactly, does exploratory behavior always seem to follow this predictable trend of seeking less novelty? The answer involves two conflicting drives: curiosity and anxiety.

究竟是什么原因导致探索行为总是遵循着这种追求较少新奇事物的可预测趋势？答案在于两种相互矛盾的驱动力：好奇心和焦虑。

You’ve probably noticed people seem fascinated by things that are new and novel. We have a natural drive toward curiosity. This is no accident. The preference for novelty is wired deep into our brains and can be triggered in an instant. If a squirrel stumbles upon a picnic basket full of sandwiches and potato chips, he has potentially discovered enough food to last him for months if he stores it properly. That is a huge benefit to his survival. But can the squirrel be sure about it? When something is totally new and novel, we don’t have much information about it. This means there is a chance the thing could deliver a big reward. So we’re programmed to be curious about novel things. The more novel something is, the more curiosity it triggers in us. Curiosity motivates us to approach things and check them out.

你可能已经注意到，人们似乎对新鲜事物充满好奇。我们天生就有一种好奇心。这并非偶然。对新奇事物的偏好深深植根于我们的大脑，并且能够瞬间被激发。如果一只松鼠偶然发现一个装满三明治和薯片的野餐篮，那么只要它妥善储存，就可能获得足够它吃上几个月的食物。这对它的生存至关重要。但松鼠真的能确定吗？当某件事物完全是新的、新奇的，我们对它知之甚少。这意味着它有可能带来巨大的回报。因此，我们天生就对新奇事物充满好奇。事物越新奇，就越能激发我们的好奇心。好奇心驱使我们去接近并探索事物。

This is why people are motivated to hear about your idea (at first) if you introduce it as new and novel and never been done before.

这就是为什么如果你把你的想法介绍成新颖独特、前所未有的，人们（一开始）会很乐意了解它的原因。

Of course, besides the potential reward, there is also potential risk inherent in novel situations. The picnic basket might not be a basket at all, but a trap that slams shut, capturing the squirrel inside. Novel situations can be dangerous. Because of this danger, we feel anxiety when we encounter novelty. This anxiety makes us more cautious. The more novel the situation is, the more anxiety we feel, and the more cautiously we act.

当然，除了潜在的回报之外，新事物也蕴含着潜在的风险。野餐篮可能根本不是篮子，而是一个会突然关闭的陷阱，把松鼠困在里面。新事物可能很危险。正因为这种危险，我们在面对新事物时会感到焦虑。这种焦虑会让我们更加谨慎。情况越新奇，我们就越焦虑，行动也越谨慎。

So how is it that novelty motivates both approach and avoidance at the same time?

那么，为什么新奇事物会同时激发趋近和回避的动机呢？

Berlyne discovered that the two conflicting drives of curiosity and anxiety are always in operation simultaneously. But at different times and in different situations, one or the other of these drives wins out. Under very novel circumstances (like a strange noise in the middle of the night) anxiety typically prevails and we experience an avoidance tendency. We stay hidden or move away. In more boring or mundane situations (like a mysterious cardboard box) curiosity more often prevails and we experience an approach tendency. We investigate.

伯林发现，好奇心和焦虑这两种相互冲突的驱动力总是同时存在。但在不同的时间和不同的情境下，其中一种驱动力会占据主导地位。在非常新奇的情况下（比如半夜听到奇怪的声音），焦虑通常会占上风，我们会表现出回避倾向。我们会躲藏起来或远离。而在比较乏味或普通的情况下（比如一个神秘的纸箱），好奇心往往会占据上风，我们会表现出接近倾向。我们会去探究。

You can see these two conflicting drives in action with the squirrel exploring a nearby empty picnic basket. The closer the squirrel gets to the mysterious basket, the more novel the situation becomes for him. At first, when he’s far away, anxiety is low and he scurries forward. But as the basket get closer and closer, the novelty level goes up and the squirrel reaches a point where anxiety and curiosity are perfectly balanced. Neither an approach nor an avoidance tendency is experienced. This is where he pauses.

你可以从松鼠探索附近空野餐篮的举动中看到这两种相互冲突的驱动力。松鼠离神秘的篮子越近，对它来说就越感到新奇。起初，当它离篮子很远时，焦虑感很低，它会迅速向前跑去。但随着篮子越来越近，新奇感也越来越强，松鼠的焦虑和好奇心达到了一种完美的平衡。此时，它既没有靠近的倾向，也没有逃避的倾向。就在这时，它停了下来。

Then he takes one more tiny step forward. And now he has crossed the line and the situation is too novel. The scale tips and anxiety wins out over curiosity. In a rush, the squirrel experiences an avoidance tendency and scurries away to hide in the bushes.

然后，他又向前迈了一小步。现在，他已经越过了界限，眼前的景象太过陌生。天平倾斜，焦虑战胜了好奇心。情急之下，松鼠本能地想要逃避，飞快地逃进了灌木丛。

As soon as he reaches the safety of the bushes (a lower level of novelty), the squirrel begins to feel more curiosity than anxiety, so he experiences an approach tendency and scampers toward the basket again, and the process repeats itself.

一旦松鼠到达灌木丛的安全区域（新奇程度降低），它就会感到更多的好奇而不是焦虑，于是它会产生接近的倾向，再次奔向篮子，然后这个过程重复发生。

On each approach the squirrel is able to get a little closer before the urge to retreat overpowers his curiosity. This is because he is gradually getting more and more exposed to the picnic basket, so his novelty sweet spot is slowly shifting closer and closer to the basket. This process might take a while, with many back-and-forth attempts, if the squirrel has never been exposed to a picnic basket before. Eventually, the novelty will wear off to the point where he can stick his head in the basket and see what’s inside.

每次接近，松鼠都能更靠近一点，但最终好奇心还是会被退缩的冲动所战胜。这是因为它逐渐接触到野餐篮，所以它感到新鲜感的临界点也慢慢地靠近了篮子。如果松鼠以前从未见过野餐篮，这个过程可能需要一段时间，需要反复尝试。最终，新鲜感会消退，它就可以把头伸进篮子里，看看里面有什么。

We’re not that different from our squirrel friend. Over millions of years these strategies have been imprinted on our DNA. We are preprogrammed to move toward things when we get bored, to increase our novelty level. Similarly, we move away from things when we get too excited, to find a lower level of novelty.

我们和松鼠朋友其实很相似。数百万年来，这些策略已经深深地烙印在我们的DNA里。当我们感到无聊时，我们会本能地靠近新鲜事物，以增加刺激感。同样地，当我们过于兴奋时，我们会远离新鲜事物，去寻找刺激感较低的环境。

At the end of the day, we’re most comfortable with things that are about average.

归根结底，我们最喜欢平庸的事物。

Ever notice how websites always seem to give you three different price levels? Companies today know that when presented with three ascending options, the vast majority of people will instinctively choose the middle one. So we strategically engineer web pages to present three options: one that is too expensive, one that is too basic or limited, and one that is “just right.”

你有没有注意到，网站总是提供三种不同的价格等级？如今，企业深谙此道：面对三个递增的选项，绝大多数人会本能地选择中间的那个。因此，我们精心设计网页，呈现三种选项：一个太贵，一个太基础或功能有限，以及一个“刚刚好”。

And time and time again customers go with the Plain Vanilla option, the most middle-of-the-road out of the three choices. My problem in Hawaii was that Mahalo Marketplace was 100 percent different from anything investors had seen before. It was in Hawaii and super profitable, which created attraction and the initial urge to approach. But it was also extremely novel, which created anxiety and the avoidance tendency—when they got a little closer, my deal scared them off.

顾客总是选择最普通的方案，也就是三种方案中最中庸的那种。我在夏威夷遇到的问题是，Mahalo Marketplace 与投资者之前见过的任何项目都截然不同。它位于夏威夷，而且利润丰厚，这本身就极具吸引力，也激发了人们最初的合作欲望。但它也极其新颖，这反而造成了焦虑和回避心理——当他们走近一些时，我的方案反而吓跑了他们。

THE ONLY WAY OUT OF A KOBAYASHI MARU

逃离小林丸号的唯一方法

How did I let Logan get me into these kinds of situations? He needed to understand that things were different for me. There was a time when I would have jumped at any deal, no matter how outlandish, if I thought it had a chance of working, but I had my family to think about now. Life had gotten a whole lot more real for me. Logan hadn’t even asked about my life. We were always too busy talking about big deals, new airplanes, and empire building.

我怎么会让洛根把我拖进这种境地？他需要明白，我的情况和以前不一样了。以前，只要我觉得有成功的可能，无论多么离谱的交易我都会毫不犹豫地抓住，但现在我得为家人着想。生活对我来说变得无比真实。洛根甚至都没问过我的生活。我们总是忙着谈论大生意、新飞机和如何打造商业帝国。

Logan had signed the contract for this deal and shaken hands with the seller. We were all in. But our research said we were about to be all out. This was the definition of an impossible situation—and exactly how I found myself drinking a beer at the KuaKua, in downtown Honolulu.

洛根已经签了合同，和卖家握了手。我们都全力以赴了。但我们的调查显示，我们即将血本无归。这简直就是个进退两难的困境——而我，也正是因为如此，才发现自己身处檀香山市中心的库阿库阿酒吧，喝着啤酒。

Logan had just signed me up for a suicide mission, and the reality of the situation was beginning to sink in. A pint of beer sounded just about right. Unfortunately, this was Logan at the table, so instead of a pint we ordered twelve bottles of Sierra Nevada Torpedo and got down to business. This meant we needed to face some difficult facts about Mahalo Marketplace.

洛根刚刚给我报了个自杀任务，我开始意识到事情的严重性。一杯啤酒听起来正合适。可惜，坐在桌旁的是洛根，所以我们没点啤酒，而是点了十二瓶塞拉内华达鱼雷啤酒，然后开始干正事。这意味着我们必须面对一些关于马哈洛市场的残酷现实。

First, my test marketing, which was typically reliable, revealed lukewarm interest from the type of investors we usually worked with. Second, we had already committed to show up in thirty days with $42 million in cash. To admit a mistake now would broadcast to everyone in our industry that we were unreliable and couldn’t be trusted to hold up our end of a deal.

首先，我以往可靠的市场测试结果显示，我们通常合作的那类投资者兴趣寥寥。其次，我们已经承诺在30天内带着4200万美元现金到场。现在承认错误，无异于向业内所有人宣告我们不可靠，无法履行承诺。

“Logan, what we’ve got here before us is a classic Kobayashi Maru,” I said between gulps of Torpedo.

“洛根，我们眼前这艘船是一艘典型的‘小林丸’号，”我一边大口喝着鱼雷啤酒一边说道。

“That test on Star Trek that nobody could pass?” he asked, looking morose.

“是《星际迷航》里那个没人能通过的测试吗？”他闷闷不乐地问道。

“Yeah, exactly. It’s a no-win situation; even Kirk failed the test twice,” I said. “Yup, this is a Kobayashi Maru all right.”

“没错，就是这样。这完全是进退两难的局面；就连柯克都考砸了两次，”我说。“没错，这简直就是个‘小林丸’号。”

The Kobayashi Maru was originally designed to test how Starfleet Academy cadets responded to a no-win scenario. It had suddenly occurred to me that Logan and I were living that fictional test, and we had to do something about it, fast. But what?

“小林丸”号最初的设计目的是为了测试星际舰队学院学员在必败情境下的反应。我突然意识到，我和洛根正身处这场虚构的测试之中，我们必须尽快采取行动。但是，我们该怎么做呢？

“So what did Captain Kirk do?” asked Logan.

“那么柯克船长做了什么？”洛根问道。

“He cheated,” I said. “He snuck into Starfleet and reprogrammed the computer.”

“他作弊了，”我说。“他潜入星际舰队，重新编程了电脑。”

“So let’s do that,” said Logan, tossing a piece of a rainbow roll into his mouth. “How can we flip the script and reprogram the game?” Over a batch of calamari and seaweed, we struggled to answer that question. What does it mean to reprogram the game when you’ve got to offload a $40 million marketplace in Hawaii?

“那就这么办吧，”洛根说着，往嘴里塞了一块彩虹卷。“我们该如何扭转乾坤，重塑游戏规则呢？”我们一边吃着鱿鱼和海藻，一边努力寻找答案。当你要出售夏威夷价值4000万美元的市场时，重塑游戏规则究竟意味着什么？

Just then, my phone rang. It was the appliance guy for the house I was building two thousand miles away, calling to go over the specs on a bunch of different kitchen appliances, starting with the refrigerator. I sighed, not yet ready to tell him it looked like we were going to have to put the whole project on hold.

就在这时，我的手机响了。是两千英里外我正在盖的房子的家电供应商打来的，他打电话来是要确认各种厨房电器的规格，先从冰箱开始。我叹了口气，还没准备好告诉他，看来我们得把整个项目搁置了。

“Hey, Vince,” I said, trying to cut him off, “I’m kind of in the middle of something right now, can’t pick out a fridge.”

“嘿，文斯，”我试图打断他，“我现在正忙着呢，没空选冰箱。”

But Vince ignored me, and kept talking through my attempted interruption. “Here’s a quick rundown of what will work for you,” he continued with excitement. “We should do the in-fridge fifteen-lens cameras so you can check what’s inside remotely from your phone and drag virtual countdown timers over the top of your food to track expiration dates. We’ll also do smart grid syncing to schedule defrost cycles. And because you travel quite a bit I’d do a twenty-five temperature zone system with speed chilling. I can get it ordered tomorrow morning and installed by end of week.”

但文斯没理会我，继续滔滔不绝地讲着，完全无视我的打断。“我给你简单介绍一下最适合你的方案，”他兴奋地说道，“我们应该在冰箱里装上15个摄像头，这样你就可以用手机远程查看里面的东西，还能在食物上方拖拽虚拟倒计时器来追踪保质期。我们还会进行智能电网同步，来安排除霜周期。考虑到你经常出差，我建议你安装一套25个温区、带速冷功能的系统。我明天早上就能下单，这周末就能安装好。”

“Vince, please. I can’t deal with this right now. Just get me the normal regular everyday refrigerator that everyone in my neighborhood gets. Whatever is standard, I’ll take it. I really don’t need a hologram countdown timer for milk.”

“文斯，拜托了。我现在没空处理这些。就给我弄个普通的、我们小区里人人都用的那种冰箱就行了。只要是标配的就行。我真的不需要什么牛奶倒计时全息投影器。”

There was a chuckle on the other end of the line. “You haven’t bought a fridge in quite a while, have you, Oren?” he said, and didn’t wait for an answer because the answer was obvious. “Well, these days for a house like yours the standard refrigerators all have speed chillers, smart sync, and reorder apps on the front digital panel. In fact, most of them have laser temperature sensors. So if you want the standard, I guess I’ll get you a totally plain-vanilla no-frills Viking. It’ll just have the linear compressors and multi-zone monitors with a backup mini-compressor. That’s it, nothing fancy. Plain vanilla. It’s $19,980,” he said quickly and hung up the phone.

电话那头传来一声轻笑。“奥伦，你很久没买冰箱了吧？”他说道，没等对方回答，因为答案显而易见。“嗯，现在像你家这样的房子，标配的冰箱都配备了速冷功能、智能同步功能，前面板上还有重新排序的应用程序。事实上，大多数冰箱都配备了激光温度传感器。所以，如果你想要标准款，那我给你买个最普通、最简陋的维京冰箱。它只有线性压缩机、多区域监控器和一个备用迷你压缩机。就这些，没什么花哨的。最普通的。售价19980美元。”说完，他迅速挂断了电话。

And then a lightbulb went on.

然后我突然灵光一闪。

“Virtual smart app countdown timers projected over the top of your food to track expiration dates?” I thought to myself. “Didn’t expect that.” Now I knew how each of the sixteen investors felt when I had tried to sell them a property that was completely different from the types of properties they were used to purchasing. No wonder they all turned me down.

“用虚拟智能应用程序在食物上方投射倒计时器来追踪保质期？”我心想，“真没想到。”现在我终于明白，当我试图向那十六位投资者推销与他们以往购买的房产类型截然不同的房产时，他们每个人的感受了。难怪他们都拒绝了我。

I had just bought a nineteen-thousand-dollar fridge I hadn’t planned to buy. What had this man said to get me to do this? I thought about it for a moment.

我刚买了一台价值一万九千美元的冰箱，这原本不在我的购买计划之内。这个人到底说了什么，让我做了这件事？我思考了一会儿。

“Normal,” Vince had said. “Standard . . . plain vanilla.” To which I had said: “OK.”

“正常，”文斯说。“标准……普通的香草味。” 我回答说：“好的。”

Immediately I started replaying the conversation over and over in my mind. What was it, exactly, that had made me buy a fridge with features I hadn’t even heard of before? The answer was almost too obvious.

我立刻开始在脑海中一遍遍地回放那段对话。究竟是什么让我买了一台拥有我以前闻所未闻的功能的冰箱？答案几乎显而易见。

He made the novel (and expensive) features seem normal.

他让那些新颖（且昂贵）的功能看起来很正常。

When I slid back into our booth after the phone call, Logan had a faraway look in his eyes, like he was in a trance. And he still hadn’t touched his spicy tuna roll. I grabbed a piece and popped it into my mouth as I tapped him on the shoulder.

挂断电话后，我回到卡座，发现洛根眼神空洞，仿佛陷入了某种恍惚状态。他的辣金枪鱼卷还一口也没动。我抓起一块，塞进嘴里，同时轻轻拍了拍他的肩膀。

“OK,” I said, “let’s do it.”

“好的，”我说，“那就这么办吧。”

“What?” Logan asked, perking up.

“什么？”洛根精神一振，问道。

“The marketplace,” I said. “I can sell it. I have an idea.”

“市场上去，”我说。“我可以卖掉它。我有个主意。”

FROM NOVEL TO THE NEW NORMAL

从小说到新常态

Imagine you just created a new cold medicine, and you find it’s 35 percent more effective than other popular cold meds on the market. Users administer it by vaping rather than taking a pill, so it doesn’t have to be swallowed. And it’s completely safe to use. Great! You’re obviously excited and your mind is going a mile a minute thinking about how popular this new product will be (and how much money you’re going to make). Some wealthy friends think it sounds like a slam dunk, so they give you a cash investment and you spend two years working out the details, and when you finally put it on store shelves . . . nobody buys it.

想象一下，你刚刚研发出一种新型感冒药，发现它比市面上其他流行的感冒药疗效高出35%。用户无需吞服药片，只需通过雾化吸入即可。而且它使用起来完全安全。太棒了！你兴奋不已，脑子里飞快地盘算着这款新产品会有多受欢迎（以及你能赚多少钱）。一些富有的朋友觉得这简直是稳赚不赔的买卖，于是他们给你投资了一笔钱，你花了两年时间完善细节，结果当你终于把产品摆上货架时……却无人问津。

Cold medicine that requires a vaporizer? No thanks. It’s just too novel.

需要雾化器的感冒药？不了，谢谢。这玩意儿太新奇了。

For the most part, customers and consumers rely on their tried-and-true suppliers or on their network of friends to suggest new products, rather than trying every new solution that hits the market. Certainly there is a small set of adopters who will try unproven products and take the risk on new ideas and technologies—but this is not true of the mainstream, middle-of-the-road consumer. In practice, too much novelty produces avoidance and not the hoped-for attraction effect.

大多数情况下，顾客和消费者更倾向于依赖他们信赖的供应商或朋友推荐新产品，而不是尝试市场上涌现的每一个新产品。当然，也有一小部分勇于尝试未经市场检验的产品，愿意承担新理念和新技术的风险——但这并不适用于主流的、不走寻常路的消费者。实际上，过多的创新反而会适得其反，无法达到预期的吸引力。

The grocery store shelves are already lined with a dozen different kinds of cold medication. Your customers have all been seeing the common brands on the shelf for years and years. They already have a medication they use, which their doctor recommended and all their friends use too. These people aren’t out looking for a new cold medication that might be one-third better. And they definitely don’t want to switch from taking pills to vaping, because they’ve never heard of anyone taking cold medicine like that before; it’s too unfamiliar.

超市货架上已经摆满了十几种不同的感冒药。你的顾客多年来一直都能看到货架上常见的那些品牌。他们已经有了自己一直在用的药，那是医生推荐的，也是朋友们都在用的。这些人不会特意去寻找一种可能效果好三分之一的新感冒药。而且他们绝对不想从吃药改成吸电子烟，因为他们以前从未听说过有人用这种方式服用感冒药；这太陌生了。

To influence people to try a new kind of anything, you first need to find somebody who is willing to listen to you talk about your new thing, whatever it is. At this stage, positioning a project or idea as completely new and original can cause a deceptive amount of interest. These people will listen to the idea. And yes, they’ll be intrigued, maybe even engaged and excited. But when it comes to making a decision, they’re likely to return to the products and ideas that are already in place and known to work—even if those products and ideas are just average.

要想影响人们尝试任何新事物，首先你需要找到愿意倾听你介绍新事物的人，无论它是什么。在这个阶段，将项目或想法包装成完全新颖原创，可能会引发意想不到的关注。这些人会听你讲解，没错，他们会被吸引，甚至可能积极参与并感到兴奋。但到了真正做决定的时候，他们很可能会回到那些已经存在且行之有效的产品和理念——即使这些产品和理念只是平庸之作。

This counterintuitive consumer behavior has frustrated thousands of entrepreneurs, who often get fired after telling their board of directors, “It’s a great product, so much better than anything else on the market . . . but we are just not selling, and are not going to hit our revenue goals this year.”

这种反直觉的消费者行为令成千上万的企业家感到沮丧，他们经常在告诉董事会“这是一个很棒的产品，比市场上任何其他产品都好得多……但我们就是卖不出去，今年也无法实现我们的收入目标”之后被解雇。

For more than a decade my colleagues and I have watched countless companies attempt to sell their products, services, and ideas to all types of buyers. Through interviews, working sessions, and consulting directly with hundreds of executives, I’ve seen the way most sales presentations go:

十多年来，我和我的同事们目睹了无数公司试图向各种类型的买家推销他们的产品、服务和理念。通过访谈、研讨会以及直接与数百位高管的咨询，我了解到大多数销售演示的运作方式：

  1. Salesperson creates excitement by pointing out new features, ideas, or technology
    销售人员通过指出新功能、新理念或新技术来激发顾客的兴趣。
  2. Introduces the offering as new and “totally different” from the competition
    将该产品定位为全新的，并与竞争对手“完全不同”。
  3. Explains that “this is a new kind of company—we do things differently”
    他解释说：“这是一家新型公司——我们的做事方式不同。”
  4. Points to industry innovation awards
    行业创新奖积分
  5. Typically quotes Steve Jobs’s commercials or keynote speech: “Here’s to the crazy ones . . . The rebels. The troublemakers. The round pegs in the square holes. The ones who see things differently.”
    他经常引用史蒂夫·乔布斯的广告或主题演讲：“敬那些疯狂的人……叛逆者。捣蛋鬼。方枘圆凿。那些以不同视角看待事物的人。”
  6. Moves to the next cliché, comparing their company to disruptive tech giants, Google, Airbnb, Etsy, or Uber (“We’re the Uber of toothpaste, really.”)
    接下来又抛出了一个老套的说法，将他们的公司比作颠覆性科技巨头，比如谷歌、Airbnb、Etsy 或 Uber（“我们就是牙膏界的 Uber，真的。”）。
  7. Tries to overcome objections and ask for the sale
    试图克服异议并促成交易

This rarely works.

这种方法很少奏效。

So why would sellers consistently choose this particular way to highlight their products? In other words, why are people seemingly preprogrammed to present their ideas as new and different? Because new and different things attract immediate attention .

那么，为什么卖家会始终选择这种特定的方式来展示他们的产品呢？换句话说，为什么人们似乎天生就倾向于将自己的想法包装成新颖独特的？因为新颖独特的事物能够立即吸引眼球。

If it sounds new, we can be sure to get a solid look, a return phone call, or an in-person meeting based on novelty. When people hear new ideas, they initially get excited, which provides social validation and gives the seller the illusion of an elevated interest level. But this rise is temporary, and when the exhilaration of new and different wears off, the desire for tried-and-true takes over, and it is surprisingly difficult to get people to actually change their behavior.

如果听起来很新颖，我们就能确保获得认真的关注、回电或基于新鲜感的面谈机会。人们听到新想法时，最初会感到兴奋，这能带来社会认同感，并让销售人员产生客户兴趣高涨的错觉。但这种兴奋只是暂时的，当新鲜感消退后，人们就会渴望久经考验的可靠事物，而要真正改变人们的行为却出乎意料地困难。
